# Part VII: Elderly Care and Population Ageing

The Maldives stands at a critical demographic inflection point. A country that only decades ago was defined by high fertility, youthful populations, and short life expectancies is now confronting the realities of rapid population ageing. With a total fertility rate that has plummeted from 6.4 children per woman in 1990 to just 1.7 by 2022 -- well below the replacement level of 2.1 -- and life expectancy at birth rising to 81.1 years, the Maldives is undergoing one of the fastest demographic transitions in the South Asian region.[^1] As of 2023, approximately 43,000 people (8.3% of the population) are aged 60 and above, and 5% are aged 65 and over.[^2] These figures, while modest in absolute terms, are projected to surge dramatically: by 2045, the proportion aged 65 and over is expected to reach 12.7%, and by 2054, projections suggest that 34.1% of the Maldivian population could be aged 60 or older -- a fivefold increase from 2014 levels.[^3] [Note: The MIPAA Statement (2022) projects 12.7% aged 65+ by 2045; separately, UN estimates project approximately 32% aged 60+ by 2050, and MoSFD/Maldives Financial Review projects 34.1% aged 60+ by 2054. The different age thresholds (60+ vs 65+) account for the apparent discrepancy.]

This chapter examines the full spectrum of elderly care and ageing policy in the Maldives: the demographic drivers, the legal and policy frameworks erected (and their implementation gaps), the pension and allowance architecture, institutional and home-based care realities, health system preparedness, the erosion of traditional family support, climate vulnerability, and the country's engagement with international frameworks such as the Madrid International Plan of Action on Ageing (MIPAA). Throughout, the analysis insists on procedural detail and frontline realities rather than mere recitation of policy documents.

---


---


# Part 1: Demographics, Population Profile, and Projections


## 7.1 Ageing Trends, Fertility Collapse, and Rising Life Expectancy


### 7.1.1 The Fertility Collapse

The Maldives' demographic trajectory is among the most compressed in global history. The total fertility rate (TFR) declined from 6.4 in the 1990 Census to 2.8 in the 2000 Census, then to 2.2 in the 2006 Census, 2.5 in Census 2014, 2.1 in the 2016-17 Demographic and Health Survey, and finally to 1.7 in Census 2022.[^4] This collapse -- from among the highest fertility rates in Asia to below replacement in the span of a single generation -- means that the base of the population pyramid is rapidly narrowing while the upper cohorts grow thicker with each passing decade. The Maldives did not experience the gradual, multi-generational fertility decline that characterised European and East Asian ageing; instead, the transition has been telescoped into roughly thirty years, leaving policymakers very little time to prepare institutional responses.

### 7.1.2 Rising Life Expectancy

Parallel to falling fertility, life expectancy has risen dramatically. Life expectancy at birth reached 81.1 years by 2023, up from approximately 46 years at independence in 1965 and 72 years in 2000.[^5] Male life expectancy stands at approximately 73 years and female at 75 years, though UN estimates for the total population are higher when factoring in recent mortality improvements.[^6] The combination of sub-replacement fertility and increasing longevity creates a classic demographic ageing scenario -- fewer young people entering the base of the population while more people survive into old age.

### 7.1.3 Current Age Structure and Projections

According to the Ministry of Social and Family Development's September 2024 presentation on population ageing, the current demographic indicators for the Maldives are as follows:[^7]

| Indicator | Value |
|---|---|
| Population aged 60+ (thousands) | 43 |
| Population aged 60+ (% of total) | 8.3% |
| Percentage of women among population aged 60+ | 46.1% |
| Percentage of women among population aged 80+ | 48.1% |
| Life expectancy at birth (years) | 81.1 |
| Median age (years) | 29.9 |
| Total fertility rate (2023) | 1.7 |

The population pyramid is undergoing a fundamental structural shift. The 0-14 age cohort, which dominated the pyramid through the 1990s, is shrinking in both absolute and relative terms. The working-age population (15-64) currently forms the largest bulge, but this cohort will itself age into the 60+ category over the coming decades. The UN Population Division's World Population Prospects 2022 project that by 2050, the 65+ cohort will expand dramatically while the 0-14 cohort will have contracted further, producing a population pyramid that more closely resembles those of upper-middle-income countries with mature demographic profiles.[^8]

The implications for dependency ratios are stark. The old-age dependency ratio (persons 65+ per 100 working-age persons) is currently low but will increase sharply. Meanwhile, the youth dependency ratio is declining, creating a temporary demographic window. If the Maldives fails to invest adequately in elderly care infrastructure, pension sustainability, and health system readiness during this window, it will face a care crisis when the large working-age cohort begins retiring in the 2030s and 2040s.

### 7.1.4 Geographic Dimensions of Ageing

Ageing does not affect all parts of the Maldives equally. The phenomenon of rural-to-urban migration, particularly of younger working-age adults to Male' and the Greater Male' Region, has left many outer atolls with disproportionately elderly populations. When young adults migrate to Male' for education and employment, they frequently leave ageing parents behind on their home islands. This creates islands where the elderly constitute a far larger share of the remaining population than national averages suggest, while simultaneously depriving those communities of the working-age adults who would traditionally serve as caregivers.

As of 2019, 47% of the population lived in the capital city Male', and this proportion is expected to increase to 64% by 2054.[^9] The concentration of services in Male' means that elderly residents of outer islands face longer travel times, higher transport costs, and reduced access to the specialised medical services they increasingly require. Meanwhile, 60% of the population in Greater Male' lives in rented accommodation, creating housing insecurity that disproportionately affects elderly people on fixed incomes.[^10]

---


## 7.2 Demographic Projections: The Path to a Super-Aged Society


### 7.2.1 The 2062 Horizon

The independent analysis platform Viraasee (2025) published the most detailed long-range demographic projections for the Maldives' elderly population: from **20,490 persons aged 65+** in 2022 to **119,246 by 2062** – a **sixfold increase**. By 2062, the dependency ratio would reach 70 per 100 working-age people (compared to approximately 20 currently), and only one in three women would be of childbearing age (down from half in 2022).[^197]

### 7.2.2 The Three Demographic Thresholds

Demographic science classifies societies through three ageing thresholds based on the proportion of the population aged 65+:

| Threshold | Definition (% aged 65+) | Maldives projected year |
|---|---|---|
| **Ageing society** | 7% | **2031** |
| **Aged society** | 14% | **2044** |
| **Super-aged society** | 21% | **2052** |

The Maldives will cross the first threshold – becoming an "ageing society" – in less than five years from the time of writing. The transition from "ageing" to "super-aged" is projected to take only 21 years (2031 to 2052), an extremely compressed timeline. For comparison, France took 115 years to make this transition, Sweden 85 years, and Japan 36 years. The Maldives' 21-year window is among the shortest projected for any country globally – reflecting the rapid speed of the fertility decline from 6.4 to 1.7 in a single generation.[^198]

### 7.2.3 Economic Impact Channels

Corporate Maldives (2024) identified three primary economic impact channels of population ageing:

**Tourism Workforce.** The tourism sector, which generates approximately 30% of GDP, relies heavily on a young workforce for resort operations. As the working-age population shrinks, the sector will face labour shortages that cannot be entirely compensated by foreign workers (who already outnumber Maldivians in many resorts).

**Fisheries.** The fishing industry, which employs a significant share of outer-island working-age men, is already experiencing an ageing workforce. As fishermen age out of physically demanding ocean work, the sector faces productivity challenges.

**Education.** Schools on small islands are already struggling to fill classrooms due to declining student numbers. The education infrastructure built for a young population will become increasingly surplus as the youth cohort shrinks, while demand for elderly care infrastructure will grow – requiring a fundamental reallocation of public investment.[^199]

---


## 7.3 The 2062 Horizon and Beyond


### 7.3.1 The UNFPA 2022-2062 Projections

The UNFPA Maldives Population Projection 2022-2062 and 2022 Census data, as analysed by Corporate Maldives (October 2025), provides the most current long-range demographic projections:

- Total Fertility Rate declined from **6 children per woman (1995)** to **1.7 (Census 2022)** – well below the replacement rate of 2.1
- Population growth continues until approximately **2050**, then contracts
- By 2062, the share of children will shrink to **13%** (down from 27% in 2022)
- **25% of the resident population** already comprises expatriate workers
- By 2062, only **one in three** Maldivian women will be of childbearing age (down from more than half in 2022)

The UN World Population Prospects 2024 provides an updated projection for the 65+ cohort: from **5.0% in 2025 to 21.5% by 2050** – a fourfold increase in just 25 years. This confirms and slightly accelerates the earlier projections used in the 2024 National Ageing Policy (which cited 34.1% aged 60+ by the 2050s using the broader 60+ threshold).[^334]

### 7.3.2 Economic Impact Channels: Updated Analysis

Corporate Maldives (October 2025) identified specific economic impact mechanisms beyond those previously documented:

**Labour market dependency on migrants:** With 25% of the resident population already being expatriate workers and the working-age Maldivian population projected to shrink, future reliance on migrant labour will intensify. However, most expatriate workers "remain outside social protection frameworks" – they do not contribute to the MRPS and will not receive the Basic Pension, creating a growing workforce segment with no retirement security in the Maldives.

**Maternity leave disparity as demographic driver:** The public sector offers 6 months' maternity leave and 1 month's paternity leave, while the private sector provides only 3 months' maternity leave – creating a childbirth disincentive in the private sector that contributes to sub-replacement fertility. This disparity accelerates demographic ageing by suppressing fertility rates.

**Education infrastructure reallocation:** Schools on smaller islands are already facing declining enrolment, with some unable to fill single classrooms. The infrastructure built for a young population (schools, playgrounds, youth centres) will become increasingly surplus, while demand for elderly care infrastructure (day centres, accessible clinics, community care) will grow – requiring a fundamental reallocation of public investment from youth infrastructure to ageing infrastructure.[^335]

---


## 7.4 Census 2022: Elderly Population Data


### 7.4.1 Updated Demographic Baseline

The Census of Population and Housing 2022, conducted by the Maldives Bureau of Statistics, provides the most current official count of the elderly population: **23,973 persons aged 65 and above (total resident population including non-Maldivians)**, constituting **6.3% of the total population** (approximately 521,000). The broader elderly category (60+) stands at approximately 43,000 (8.3%). The old-age dependency ratio – persons aged 65+ per 100 working-age persons (15-64) – was **7 per 100 in 2022**, compared to 8 per 100 in 2014. The slight decrease in the old-age dependency ratio between censuses reflects the temporary demographic dividend as the large working-age cohort from the high-fertility era of the 1980s-1990s swells the denominator. This ratio will begin rising sharply from the late 2030s as this cohort ages past 65.[^282]

---


## 7.5 Census 2022: The Definitive Elderly Population Profile


### 7.5.1 Total Elderly Population and Age Distribution

The Census 2022, supplemented by the Maldives Bureau of Statistics' International Day of Elderly Persons statistical release (October 2024), provides the most detailed demographic profile of the Maldivian elderly population available. The total resident Maldivian population aged 65 and above was **20,553 (Maldivian citizens only)** – constituting 5% of the total Maldivian population of 382,751. The gender split was nearly equal: 10,530 males (51.2%) and 10,023 females (48.8%).[^422]

The age distribution within the elderly cohort reveals an important structure:

| Age Group | Number | Percentage | Male | Female |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| 65-74 | 13,003 | 63% | 6,668 | 6,335 |
| 75-84 | 5,774 | 28% | 2,839 | 2,935 |
| 85+ | 1,776 | 9% | 1,023 | 753 |

The 85+ cohort – the "oldest old" – numbered 1,776, a small but rapidly growing group with the highest care needs and the most intensive health service utilisation. Notably, males outnumber females in the 85+ group (1,023 vs 753), which is unusual by international standards where women typically predominate among the oldest old due to longer life expectancy. This may reflect historical mortality patterns specific to the Maldives or data quality issues in age reporting among the oldest cohorts.[^422]

### 7.5.2 Geographic Distribution: Elderly by Atoll

The Census 2022 data, as presented in the Elderly Persons Day 2024 statistical release, provides the first comprehensive atoll-level breakdown of the 65+ population:

| Atoll/City | 65+ Population |
|---|---|
| **Male' (Greater Male' Area)** | **6,699** |
| **Addu City (Seenu)** | **1,802** |
| Haa Dhaal Atoll | 1,303 |
| Raa Atoll | 1,018 |
| Gaaf Dhaal Atoll | 985 |
| Haa Alif Atoll | 963 |
| Laamu Atoll | 807 |
| Shaviyani Atoll | 799 |
| Noonu Atoll | 703 |
| Gnaviyani Atoll | 698 |
| Gaaf Alif Atoll | 658 |
| Baa Atoll | 615 |
| Thaa Atoll | 594 |
| Kaafu Atoll (excl. Male') | 588 |
| Alif Dhaalu Atoll | 513 |
| Lhaviyani Atoll | 481 |
| Meemu Atoll | 353 |
| Alif Alif Atoll | 337 |
| Dhaalu Atoll | 302 |
| Faafu Atoll | 224 |
| Vaavu Atoll | 111 |

This data reveals critical planning implications. **Male' houses 32.6% of all elderly** (6,699 of 20,553 Maldivian citizens aged 65+), meaning two-thirds of the elderly Maldivian population lives outside the capital – dispersed across 20 administrative divisions where health services, social support, and geriatric capacity are minimal or absent. Addu City, with 1,802 elderly, is the second-largest concentration. The atolls with the smallest elderly populations – Vaavu (111), Faafu (224), Dhaalu (302) – face the challenge of providing elderly services to very small populations dispersed across multiple islands. The atolls with the largest elderly populations outside the two cities – Haa Dhaal (1,303), Raa (1,018), Gaaf Dhaal (985) – are in the northern and southern extremities of the archipelago, furthest from Male'-based tertiary healthcare.[^423]

### 7.5.3 Disability Among the Elderly

The Census 2022 documented that **7,402 elderly persons (36% of the 65+ population) have a disability** – with 3,378 males and 4,024 females. The higher female disability prevalence (40.1% of elderly women vs 32.1% of elderly men) aligns with international patterns where women's longer life expectancy translates into more years lived with disability. Disability was predominantly in **walking, seeing, and remembering** – the three functional domains most critical for independent living. This 36% disability prevalence rate among the elderly, when combined with the finding that only 16% of elderly are in the labour force and 84% are outside it, confirms that the vast majority of disabled elderly persons are dependent on family or institutional support for daily functioning.[^424]

### 7.5.4 Marital Status and the Widowhood Gender Gap

| Status | Overall | Male | Female |
|---|---|---|---|
| Married | 60% | 78.3% | 40.2% |
| Widowed | 29% | 11.1% | 47.4% |
| Divorced | 11% | 9.4% | 12.0% |
| Never married | 1% | 1.2% | 0.4% |

The most striking finding is the **widowhood gender gap**: 47.4% of elderly women are widowed compared to only 11.1% of elderly men. This means nearly half of all elderly women in the Maldives have lost their spouse – with profound implications for social support, economic security, and mental health. The Moosa (2019) finding that male respondents view their wives as their primary emotional support suggests that the 11.1% of widowed elderly men face acute emotional vulnerability. For the 47.4% of widowed elderly women, the loss of a husband typically brings not only grief but also reduced income (particularly if the husband was the formal sector earner with MRPS savings) and potentially reduced social status within the household hierarchy.[^425]

### 7.5.5 Educational Attainment

| Level | Overall | Male | Female |
|---|---|---|---|
| Primary education | 72% | 67% | 76% |
| Secondary education | 12% | 14% | 11% |
| Certificate/Diploma | 7% | 9% | 4% |
| Degree and above | 1% | 2% | 0% |
| Pre-school education | 2% | 2% | 2% |
| Level not stated | 6% | 5% | 6% |

More than half of the elderly have only primary education, and **one in five elderly persons has never attended school**. The gender gap in education is particularly pronounced at higher levels: 2% of elderly men hold a degree or above, compared to effectively 0% of elderly women. Only 4% of elderly women hold a certificate or diploma, versus 9% of elderly men. These education levels directly limit elderly persons' ability to access written health information, navigate digital government services, complete pension application forms, or engage with formal complaint mechanisms. The Nazra (2018) finding that pension forms must be filled out by family members because the elderly person "cannot do it myself" is explained by this education profile.[^426]

### 7.5.6 Employment and Labour Force Participation

The Census 2022 confirmed that only **16% of the elderly population participates in the labour force** (male: 22%, female: 9%). A total of 3,049 elderly persons (15%) are employed, 178 (1%) are unemployed, and 17,326 (84%) are entirely outside the labour force. The gender gap is stark: 91% of elderly women are outside the labour force compared to 78% of elderly men.

Among the 3,049 employed elderly, the sectoral distribution reveals a largely informal, subsistence-oriented workforce:
- **Manufacturing** (including traditional crafts like shorteats and fangi vinun): 28% (male 15%, female 61%)
- **Agriculture, forestry, and fishery**: 20% (male 24%, female 10%)
- **Wholesale/retail trade**: 19% (male 24%, female 6%)

The dominance of manufacturing among elderly female workers (61%) reflects the continuation of traditional home-based production activities – making food items and handicrafts – rather than formal manufacturing employment. This informal, home-based work generates income but does not contribute to MRPS pension savings, confirming the pension vulnerability pathway described in §7.31.[^427]

---


## 7.6 The UNFPA Population Projections 2014-2054: The Demographic Cliff in Detail


### 7.6.1 Report Context and Methodology

The UNFPA publication "Maldives Population Projections 2014-2054: Assumptions and Results Analysis," produced by the National Bureau of Statistics with technical guidance from Professor John F. May and Mr. Andreas Demmke, and produced using the DemProj module of the Spectrum platform, provides the definitive medium-term demographic projections for the Maldives. Based on the 2014 Population and Housing Census, the projections assume: TFR declining from 2.46 (2014) to 1.9 (2054); life expectancy at birth rising from 73.1 years (male) and 74.8 years (female) in 2014 to 83.3 and 83.6 years respectively by 2054; a sex ratio at birth converging from 108 (2014) to the international standard of 105 by 2054; and net internal migration to the Male' area of approximately 2,200 persons annually.[^428]

### 7.6.2 The Elderly Population Trajectory: A Fivefold Increase

The projections document the scale of the demographic transformation confronting the Maldives:

| Year | Elderly 65+ (Maldivian) | Share of Population | Old Dependency Ratio | Ageing Index |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| 2014 | 16,377 | 5% | 7 | 17 |
| 2020 | 18,178 | 5% | 7 | 17 |
| 2025 | 24,216 | 6% | – | – |
| 2030 | 31,783 | 7% | 11 | 30 |
| 2035 | 39,870 | 9% | – | – |
| 2040 | 48,432 | 10% | 14 | 49 |
| 2045 | 58,486 | 11% | – | – |
| 2050 | 74,516 | 14% | 20 | 75 |
| 2054 | 88,963 | 16% | 24 | 90 |

The elderly population is projected to grow from 16,377 in 2014 to **88,963 by 2054** – a **5.4-fold increase** in 40 years. The old dependency ratio (elderly per 100 working-age persons) rises from 7 to 24, meaning the number of elderly persons that each working-age person must support triples. The ageing index (elderly per 100 children) rises from 17 to 90 – approaching parity between the elderly and child populations.[^429]

### 7.6.3 The 80+ Cohort: A Population Larger Than Today's Entire Elderly

The detailed age breakdown within the elderly population reveals the most dramatic shift at the oldest ages:

| Age Group | 2014 | 2030 | 2050 | 2054 |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| 65-69 | 4,976 | 13,284 | 27,854 | 32,008 |
| 70-74 | 4,885 | 9,751 | 18,386 | 23,811 |
| 75-79 | 3,733 | 4,345 | 13,139 | 15,098 |
| 80+ | 3,013 | 4,940 | 16,025 | 19,028 |

By 2054, the 80+ cohort alone (19,028) will be **larger than the entire 65+ population in 2014** (16,607). This has profound implications for health system planning: the 80+ age group has the highest rates of multi-morbidity, cognitive decline, functional dependency, and need for long-term care. A health system that currently struggles to serve 16,000 elderly will need to serve 89,000 – with the most care-intensive subset (80+) growing from 3,000 to 19,000.[^430]

### 7.6.4 The Urbanisation Dimension

The projections project that **64% of the total resident population will live in the Male' area by 2054**, up from 38% in 2014. The government's stated policy objective is to consolidate 70% of the population into the Greater Male' Area by 2050. This means that an increasing proportion of the elderly population will be concentrated in the urban environment where Nazra (2018) and Azeez et al. (2020) documented the highest rates of depression, loneliness, and social confinement. Conversely, the atolls' share declines from 62% to 36% – but the remaining atoll population will be disproportionately elderly, as young adults continue to migrate to Male'.[^431]

---


## 7.7 WHO NCD Mortality Data: The Elderly Disease Burden in Context


Non-communicable diseases account for approximately **85% of all deaths** in the Maldives according to WHO SEARO data. The cause-of-death breakdown reveals the disease environment in which elderly Maldivians live and die:

| Cause of Death | Share of All Deaths |
|---|---|
| Cardiovascular diseases | 38% |
| Cancer | 18% |
| Other NCDs | 16% |
| Chronic respiratory diseases | 9% |
| Diabetes | 6% |
| Communicable, maternal, perinatal, nutritional | 13% |
| Injuries | ~2% |

The probability of premature NCD death (between ages 30 and 70) has declined significantly – from **25.7% in 2000 to 11.6% in 2019** – reflecting improvements in healthcare access and NCD management. However, this decline in premature mortality means more people survive into old age with chronic conditions, increasing the cumulative NCD burden among the 65+ population. One in four people aged 15-65 are smokers, contributing to the future elderly disease burden. WHO notes a critical data quality caveat: Maldives has **"low completeness and/or severe quality issues with cause-of-death assignment"**, meaning these estimates should be used with caution and the true disease burden may differ from recorded statistics.[^436]

---


# Part 2: Legal and Policy Framework


## 7.8 Constitutional Provisions and Legal Architecture


### 7.8.1 Constitutional Provisions

The Constitution of the Maldives (2008) does not contain specific provisions addressing the rights of older persons as a distinct group. However, several general constitutional guarantees apply to the elderly. The right to life and the protection of life under law (Article 21), the prohibition of discrimination (Article 17), the right to adequate food and water (Article 23(a)), the right to clothing and housing (Article 23(b)), and the right to social security (Article 35) collectively form the constitutional basis upon which elderly welfare policies are constructed. The right to social security under Article 35 has been particularly important in undergirding the pension framework.[^11]

### 7.8.2 The National Elderly Policy (2018)

The National Elderly Policy, endorsed in 2018 under the Ministry of Gender, Family and Social Services (MoGFSS), represents the first dedicated policy instrument for older persons in Maldivian history. Developed with the stated objective of addressing the socio-economic challenges faced by older persons and promoting ways to ensure their protection, participation, and contribution as responsible members within families and communities, the policy is framed within a rights-based architecture.[^12]

The policy articulates six fundamental rights of older persons:[^13]

1. **Right to life with dignity** (with freedom from discrimination)
2. **Right to health**
3. **Right to work**
4. **Right to property and inheritance**
5. **Right to social security**
6. **Right to freedom from violence**

The policy is structured around four core principles that underpin all interventions:[^14]

- **Protection of Rights** -- ensuring that the legal and institutional framework safeguards the fundamental rights of older persons
- **Independence and Participation** -- promoting the autonomy of older persons and their active engagement in family and community life
- **Health and Well-being** -- ensuring access to healthcare services and promoting healthy ageing
- **Preparatory Measures** -- planning for the demographic transition and building institutional capacity before the ageing population reaches critical mass

These principles are drawn from international frameworks including the UN Principles for Older Persons (1991), the Constitution of the Maldives (2008), the UN Decade of Healthy Ageing (2021-2030), and the 2030 Agenda for Sustainable Development.[^15]

### 7.8.3 National Plan of Action on Older Persons (2019-2023)

Based on the National Elderly Policy's six policy areas, the National Plan of Action on Older Persons was endorsed in 2019. This operational document translates the policy's broad commitments into 34 specific priority actions to be undertaken in collaboration with 10 relevant stakeholders across government. The Plan identifies four policy areas:[^16]

1. **Care and Support** -- expanding residential and community-based care options
2. **Independence and Participation** -- promoting active ageing and social inclusion
3. **Healthcare and Wellbeing** -- strengthening health services for older persons
4. **Preparation for Ageing** -- building systems and capacity in advance of demographic pressure

The Plan was designed to be implemented over the period 2019-2023, with a monitoring framework built into the action plan. However, implementation was severely disrupted by the COVID-19 pandemic. As the government acknowledged in its 2021 MIPAA survey response, "since the Action plan was launched in November 2019, no monitoring has been done since the pandemic situation."[^17] The plan was to be renewed after the development of a dedicated law on the elderly, which was still in draft form as of 2022.

### 7.8.4 The Absence of a Dedicated Elderly Law

One of the most significant gaps in the Maldivian legal framework is the absence of a dedicated law on the rights and protection of older persons. While the Domestic Violence Act (3/2012) prohibits violence in domestic settings and the Social Protection Act (2/2014) provides for certain allowances, neither instrument specifically addresses the unique legal needs of older persons or mentions their rights explicitly.[^18]

As noted in the MIPAA survey response, "A policy is developed exclusively for the elderly where gender and disability concerns are addressed, however, we don't have any legislation which has addressed concerns of older persons explicitly."[^19] Work on drafting a law on the elderly was reported to be underway as of 2021, with a consultant hired and the first draft planned for submission to the Majlis (Parliament) in the first quarter of 2022. As of the time of writing, this law has not yet been enacted, leaving the elderly population without the specific legal protections that a dedicated statute would provide.

This legislative gap has practical consequences. Without a specific law, there is no statutory basis for mandatory reporting of elder abuse, no legal definition of elder neglect distinct from general domestic violence, no requirement for age-friendly design in public infrastructure, and no legal mandate for geriatric training of healthcare workers. The elderly remain covered by general provisions that were not designed with their specific vulnerabilities in mind.

### 7.8.5 Other Relevant Legislation

Several other pieces of legislation touch upon elderly welfare without being specifically designed for it:

- **Maldives Pension Act (8/2009)**: Establishes the Old-Age Basic Pension and Maldives Retirement Pension Scheme (discussed in detail in Section 7.3).
- **Social Protection Act (2/2014)**: Provides the framework for social protection allowances administered by the National Social Protection Agency (NSPA), though it does not specify older persons as a distinct beneficiary category.
- **National Social Health Insurance Act (15/2011)**: Establishes the Aasandha universal health insurance scheme, which provides healthcare coverage to all citizens including the elderly.
- **Family Act (4/2000)**: Contains provisions regarding family obligations and inheritance that affect elderly family members.
- **Domestic Violence Act (3/2012)**: Prohibits violence within domestic settings, applicable to elder abuse situations but not specifically designed for them.

### 7.8.6 Institutional Architecture

The institutional architecture for elderly affairs has been characterised by fragmentation and limited dedicated capacity. The primary coordinating body is the **National Committee on Healthy Ageing and Rights of Elderly**, established in 2019 at the technical level within the Ministry (then MoGFSS, now the Ministry of Social and Family Development).[^20] The committee's function is to monitor and provide technical support for the successful implementation of the National Elderly Action Plan (NEAP 2019-2023).

However, the government's own assessment in the MIPAA survey reveals significant capacity constraints. When asked about challenges to legislation and policy implementation, the Maldives reported that the following were "always" present challenges:[^21]

- **Lack of financial resources** dedicated to population ageing issues ("Limited Budget dedicated for this area")
- **Lack of data** on older persons at national and subnational levels ("Data collection is expensive with limited capacity/resources")
- **Lack of overall political support** ("Other pressing issues take precedence when designing policies and dedication from government")
- **Lack of cross-ministerial coordination** ("overarching issue of lack of coordination among government sector and private sector partners")
- **Lack of public awareness** ("Limited budget and stakeholders to work. Information on issues faced by older person is limited. Lack of data in the area creates gaps in designing interventions")

These self-reported challenges paint a picture of an institutional framework that exists on paper but lacks the resources, political priority, and coordination mechanisms necessary for effective implementation.

---


## 7.9 The 2024 Revised National Ageing Policy


### 7.9.1 National Elderly Policy: 2018 to 2024 Revision

The original National Elderly Policy (gazetted 2017, formally launched October 2017, endorsed 2018) represented the Maldives' first dedicated policy instrument for older persons. However, by 2024, the government recognised that the rapidly evolving demographic situation required a comprehensive policy revision. The Ministry of Social and Family Development, with technical support from UN ESCAP, developed a revised **National Ageing Policy** (Final Draft 28 August 2024, with comments incorporated 9 September 2024) that substantially expanded the scope, evidence base, and implementation architecture of the original.[^115]

### 7.9.2 Situational Analysis

The 2024 policy draft's situational analysis documented the five key drivers of ageing in the Maldives:

**1. Rapid Pace of Ageing.** The policy cited UN World Population Prospects 2022 data showing 8.3% of the population aged 60+ as of 2023, with projections to 34.1% by 2050 – "one of the fastest ageing societies in the world." Life expectancy had risen from 71 years in 2000 to 79.6 years in 2019 (per the 2024 National Ageing Policy citing 2019 data; UN DESA World Population Prospects 2022 reports life expectancy at birth of 81.1 years by 2023), with healthy life expectancy increasing from 62.4 to 70 years over the same period. Women above 80 constituted 48.1% of the older-old population, signalling a feminisation of advanced ageing.[^116]

**2. Rise of Non-Communicable Diseases.** With 46% of elderly having hypertension, 28% diabetes, and 45% some form of disability, NCDs dominated the elderly health profile. The top hospital admission causes across age cohorts (65-74, 75-85, and 85+) were detailed, showing the progressive shift from ischaemic heart disease to cerebrovascular disease and lower respiratory infections with advancing age.[^117]

**3. Decreasing Family Size and Changing Household Living Arrangements.** While extended family households had actually risen from 67.9% (2009) to 73.6% (2017), average household size had decreased from 7.1 to 6 persons – suggesting that families were sharing space but in smaller units. The fertility decline from 6.4 (Census 1990) to 1.7 (Census 2022) meant that future elderly persons would have fewer adult children available as potential caregivers, a structural shift with profound implications for the traditional family care model.[^118]

**4. Labour Force Participation.** Only 20.9% of elderly labour force participation was in formal employment; 89.9% was in the informal sector. Informal sector employment was 59% female and 41% male, with 43% of informal sector workers being self-employed women. The policy noted that women constituted 59% of the overall informal sector workforce, creating pension adequacy concerns given the contributory MRPS's formal-sector design.[^119]

**5. Impact of Rapid Urbanisation and Rural-to-Urban Migration.** With 47% of the population living in Male' (2019), projected to increase to 64% by 2054, and 60% in rented accommodation, the urban ageing challenge was intensifying. The policy recognised that urbanisation was simultaneously concentrating elderly in cramped housing and depleting outer islands of the working-age population that traditionally cared for elderly parents.[^120]

### 7.9.3 Expanded Guiding Principles

The 2024 revision introduced nine guiding principles – significantly more detailed than the four principles of the 2018 policy:

1. Longevity should be appreciated, protected, and honoured
2. Older persons have rights to a thriving, productive longer life
3. Older persons have the choice to age in a place most beneficial to their well-being
4. Population ageing must be mainstreamed in all government planning
5. Families and caregivers need recognition and support
6. "It takes a village" – community-wide responsibility for elderly welfare
7. Traditional customs and institutions must be recognised and integrated
8. Attention to the specific needs of older persons with physical and mental disability
9. An enabling built environment is an important contributor to healthy ageing[^121]

### 7.9.4 Four Policy Areas

The revised policy was structured around four policy areas, each with specific goals and strategies:

**Policy Area I: Development, Prevention, Protection, and Lifelong Learning** (6 Goals). This area addressed mainstreaming ageing in all development planning; protecting older persons from discrimination, violence, abuse, and harm; ensuring rights protection through legislation, community infrastructure, and service delivery; promoting knowledge transfer, lifelong learning, and meaningful participation; increasing research and data collection for evidence-based decisions; and communication strategies on social protection. Strategies included establishing a coordination mechanism, creating Older Persons Associations, addressing ageism, decentralising social services, extending social protection to women and persons with disabilities, and building academic research collaboration on ageing.[^122]

**Policy Area II: Optimal Health and Well-being** (4 Goals). Goals included promoting a healthy and active life span across all life stages; enabling ageing-in-place through decentralised, age-friendly communities with integrated health-social infrastructure; ensuring unhindered, timely, equitable access to care; and emergency/crisis preparedness for older persons. Strategies encompassed establishing geriatric and palliative care services in health facilities, building integrated community-based primary health-social long-term care systems, creating financial and health-social service accessibility mechanisms, and including older persons in climate change adaptation planning.[^123]

**Policy Area III: Enabling and Supportive Environment** (4 Goals). This area focused on age-friendly communities with accessible infrastructure and intergenerational linkages; comprehensive support for unpaid family caregivers (especially women); digital empowerment and technological skills for older persons; and geriatric and gerontological competencies across all sectors. Strategies included universal design in urban and island planning, caregiver training and support systems, digital platforms for essential information, and geriatric/gerontology curriculum for health professionals and social workers.[^124]

**Policy Area IV: Income Security for Older Persons** (2 Goals). Goals were sustainable and equitable income security mechanisms, and social protection/security schemes for caregivers. Strategies included regular pension system reviews, inflation-indexed benefit levels, development of National Transfer Accounts, and extension of social protection to unpaid women caregivers – the first explicit recognition that caregiving is economically productive work deserving of pension coverage.[^125]

### 7.9.5 Implementation Mechanism

The 2024 policy designated the Ministry of Social and Family Development as the coordinating body, using a whole-of-government and community approach. It called for a 5-year Action Plan with defined roles for government agencies, civil society, the private sector, and local communities, supported by dedicated funding and a regular monitoring and evaluation mechanism. International frameworks referenced included MIPAA, the UN Decade of Healthy Ageing (2021-2030), the 2030 Agenda, the Kuala Lumpur Declaration on Ageing (2015), the WHO Active Ageing Policy Framework (2003), and the Dahlgren-Whitehead Rainbow Model of socioecological determinants of health.[^126]

---


## 7.10 The 2024 National Ageing Policy: Full Analysis


### 7.10.1 Policy Revision Context

The 2024 National Ageing Policy represents a comprehensive revision of the 2018 National Elderly Policy, retitled to reflect the shift from an "elderly" focus to an "ageing" focus – aligning with WHO's emphasis on population ageing as a whole-of-society process rather than an elderly-specific welfare concern. The final draft, dated 28 August 2024 with comments incorporated on 9 September 2024, was developed with the explicit purpose of: (a) aligning the policy to the outcomes of the Fourth Review and Appraisal of MIPAA in Asia and the Pacific and the 2030 Agenda; (b) ensuring it covers recent and emerging trends in population dynamics and health/social care needs; and (c) providing an integrated, coordinated platform for all policies and systems already developed or being developed.[^231]

### 7.10.2 Key Situational Analysis Data

The policy document provided several data points that consolidate findings from multiple sources:

**Household structure changes:** Extended family households (three-generation households of children, parents, and grandparents) increased from 67.9% in 2009 to 73.6% in 2017, but household size decreased from 7.1 to 6 persons over the same period. This paradox – more multigenerational households but smaller households – reflects the compression of families into smaller living spaces. With fewer children per family, the caregiving pool within each household shrinks even as the multigenerational structure persists.[^232]

**Labour force participation:** The formal employment rate among the elderly was only 20.9% (male 25.5%, female 16.2%). The informal sector employment rate was a striking 89.9% (male 84%, female 99.1%). The near-total informality of female employment (99.1%) means that virtually all women reaching retirement age will have accumulated negligible MRPS savings and will depend entirely on the MVR 5,000 Basic Pension. The policy explicitly noted that "a policy on ageing would help to regulate conditions for those, especially women working in the informal sector ensuring that sufficient social protection and safety nets are afforded to them."[^233]

**Life expectancy refinement:** The 2024 National Ageing Policy cited life expectancy at birth as 79.6 years in 2019 (i.e., life expectancy was 79.6 in 2019, rising to 81.1 by 2023 per UN DESA World Population Prospects 2022), with healthy life expectancy at 70 years – meaning the average Maldivian lives approximately 9.6 years in poor health before death.[^234]

### 7.10.3 The Four Policy Areas and Sixteen Goals

The revised policy established a comprehensive framework of four policy areas containing sixteen goals:

**Policy Area I – Development, Prevention, Protection and Life-long Learning** (6 goals):
- Goal 1: Mainstream ageing in all development planning through age-disaggregated data collection
- Goal 2: Protect older persons against all forms of discrimination, violence, abuse, and harm
- Goal 3: Protect and promote elderly rights through legislation, community social infrastructure, and service delivery
- Goal 4: Create opportunities for knowledge transfer, lifelong learning, and meaningful participation
- Goal 5: Increase research and evidence-based decision-making on ageing
- Goal 6: Develop communication strategies regarding social protection for older persons

**Policy Area II – Optimal Health and Well-being** (4 goals):
- Goal 1: Achieve a healthy and active life span enabling meaningful and productive ageing
- Goal 2: Enable ageing-in-place through age-friendly decentralised communities with integrated health-social infrastructure
- Goal 3: Ensure unhindered, timely, fair, and equitable access to care for older persons and their caregivers
- Goal 4: Ensure adequate preparation and access to appropriate aid during emergencies/crises for all older persons

**Policy Area III – Enabling and Supportive Environment** (4 goals):
- Goal 1: Develop age-friendly communities, safe built environments, and accessible infrastructure
- Goal 2: Develop comprehensive support mechanisms for unpaid family caregivers, especially women
- Goal 3: Enable digital empowerment and technological skills learning for older persons
- Goal 4: Develop and incorporate geriatric and gerontological competencies across all sectors

**Policy Area IV – Income Security for Older Persons** (2 goals):
- Goal 1: Ensure sustainable and equitable income security mechanisms
- Goal 2: Establish comprehensive social protection programmes and security schemes for caregivers[^235]

### 7.10.4 Theoretical Framework: The Socioecological Rainbow Model

The policy adopted the Dahlgren and Whitehead (1991) socioecological rainbow model as its organising theoretical framework – a systematic approach showing the relationship between approaches to health and "total, whole-of-life development, including the spiritual dimension." This framework positions individual biological factors at the centre, surrounded by concentric layers of individual lifestyle factors, social and community networks, living and working conditions, and general socioeconomic, cultural, and environmental conditions. The selection of this model represented a significant intellectual advance over the 2018 policy, which had articulated policy areas without a unifying theoretical framework. It positions ageing as a social determinant of health issue rather than a medical or welfare problem alone.[^236]

### 7.10.5 Nine Guiding Principles

The policy articulated nine guiding principles that expanded on the 2018 policy's foundation:

1. Longevity is hard-earned and should be appreciated, protected, and honoured in all of society.
2. Older persons have rights to a thriving, productive longer life with optimised health and social connections, and opportunities for life-long learning.
3. Older persons have the choice to age in a place most beneficial to their well-being.
4. Population ageing needs to be understood holistically and mainstreamed in all aspects of government planning, implementation, and monitoring.
5. Families and caregivers need recognition and support to provide the best care.
6. "It takes a village to raise a child, likewise it takes a village to enable successful and meaningful ageing."
7. Traditional customs and institutions must be recognised.
8. Special attention to the needs of older persons with physical and mental disability.
9. An enabling built environment is an important contributor to successful and meaningful population ageing.[^237]

### 7.10.6 Implementation Structure

The Ministry of Social and Family Development was designated as the coordinating body responsible for overseeing implementation, ensuring cross-sectoral alignment, and facilitating communication among stakeholders. The implementation strategy required: defined roles and responsibilities for government agencies, civil society, private sector, and local communities; dedicated funding and resources; ongoing stakeholder engagement and capacity building; and a regular monitoring and evaluation mechanism. The policy called for a 5-year Action Plan – which materialised as the National Action Plan on Ageing 2025-2028, launched in 2025.[^238]

---


## 7.11 The 2024 National Ageing Policy: Detailed Goals and Strategies


### 7.11.1 Policy Area I: Detailed Strategies

The 2024 National Ageing Policy's Policy Area I (Development, Prevention, Protection and Life-long Learning) includes six goals with the following specific strategies:

**Goal 1 – Mainstreaming Strategy:** Establish a coordination mechanism to monitor and mainstream the policy on ageing across government. Establish community-level **Older Persons Associations** across the country, supported by the government. Address ageism to enable the development of a society for all ages. This strategy explicitly calls for government-supported elderly community organisations – a significant commitment given that currently only 5 CSOs nationally address elderly issues.

**Goal 2 – Prevention and Protection Strategy:** Develop legal and administrative mechanisms and **decentralised social services** that protect and uphold the rights and wellbeing of older persons. Develop policies, procedures, and manuals for advocacy and decentralised social services for older persons.

**Goal 3 – Rights Strategy:** Ensure robust social protection is in place among older persons, **especially women and older persons with disabilities**.

**Goal 4 – Meaningful Participation Strategy:** Create and develop mechanisms and programmes to drive **life-long learning** for older persons. Create opportunities and **volunteering platforms** for older persons that enable them to contribute and engage in the community. This directly addresses the finding from Nazra (2018) that none of the eight respondents had any employment or volunteering opportunities.

**Goal 5 – Research Strategy:** Increase collaboration with academic and research institutions to conduct research on ageing. This addresses the severe evidence gap documented throughout this chapter.

**Goal 6 – Communication Strategy:** Conduct awareness and educational programmes to all sectors in society regarding social protection and security for older persons and their family caregivers.[^303]

### 7.11.2 Policy Area II: Detailed Strategies

**Goal 1 – Healthy and Active Life Span:** Develop and strengthen key services across the life-health-social spectrum to ensure universal access to health services. Develop **preventive health care through promoting self-care efficacy** with health education, functional fitness, and mental wellness training for older persons and their families. Advocate for **special geriatric/palliative care** and programmes in health care facilities.

**Goal 2 – Ageing-in-Place:** Develop and implement a comprehensive network of **integrated community-based primary health-social long-term care system**. This strategy envisions the creation of a system that does not currently exist in any form in the Maldives.

**Goal 3 – Accessibility:** Build financial and health-social service mechanisms in the community to enable accessibility to holistic care. Strengthen protection mechanisms to adequately address and prevent the abuse and exploitation of older persons.

**Goal 4 – Crisis Preparedness:** Include older persons in **all aspects of preparation in crisis and emergency management**. Include older persons' needs as a priority during crises and emergencies. Ensure the coordination agency on National Policy on Ageing is **represented in all climate change adaptation and mitigation planning and actions** – a specific cross-sectoral linkage between ageing policy and climate policy that is not found in the ageing policy frameworks of most regional comparators.[^304]

### 7.11.3 Policy Area III: Detailed Strategies

**Goal 1 – Age-Friendly Built Environment:** Incorporate **inclusive universal designs and national spatial planning** in all blueprints of urban and island planning, housing, and transportation, with specific consideration of the needs of older persons. Promote sensitisation and orientation to healthy ageing in training programmes across all industries.

**Goal 2 – Family Caregiver Support:** Develop and provide **comprehensive caregivers' training** for both formal and informal caregivers. Promote sensitisation and training programmes to equip caregivers with skills and knowledge. Develop a **"robust caregiver support system to enable joyful caregiving"** – language that acknowledges the emotional toll of caregiving and the need for support systems that sustain caregiver well-being.

**Goal 3 – Digital Empowerment:** Develop and promote programmes on digital technologies for older persons. Develop **specialised digital platforms** for older persons to enable access to essential information including health and financial information. This addresses the COVID-19 digital literacy crisis documented in §7.35.

**Goal 4 – Competencies Development:** Incorporate **generic and specialised geriatric and gerontology training** in the curriculum of health professionals, social workers, community and family health workers. This strategy would, if implemented, begin to address the complete absence of geriatric training capacity in the Maldivian health system.[^305]

### 7.11.4 Policy Area IV: Detailed Strategies

**Goal 1 – Income Security:** Conduct **regular reviews of the pension system** including its long-term sustainability and ensure access and adequate benefit levels for older persons. **Adjust benefit levels based on the inflation index** – a specific commitment to indexation that, if implemented, would address the purchasing power erosion documented in §7.68.1. Ensure income security reaches targeted beneficiaries. Improve redistribution elements using **National Transfer Accounts (NTAs)** to ensure policy effectively addresses needs and supports evidence-based decision-making.

**Goal 2 – Caregiver Social Protection:** **Extend social protection and social security to family caregivers, especially (unpaid) woman caregivers**, to recognise their contribution to family caregiving. This is the most explicit commitment in Maldivian policy history to addressing the gendered caregiving burden through social protection mechanisms – directly responding to the finding that 69% of bedridden elderly caregivers are female and that 99.1% of female employment is informal.[^306]

---


## 7.12 The National Ageing Policy Draft 2024: Situational Analysis Detail


### 7.12.1 The Inpatient Admission Data by Elderly Age Cohort

The 2024 National Ageing Policy draft included detailed tables (Table 2 and Table 2A) drawn from the Ministry of Health's Maldives Health Statistics 2020, showing the leading causes of hospital admission for three elderly cohorts. The top five causes shift significantly across age groups:

For the **65-74 age group**, admissions are dominated by cardiovascular emergencies and chronic disease management.

For the **75-85 age group**, cerebrovascular conditions (strokes) and respiratory diseases become increasingly prominent alongside cardiovascular conditions.

For the **85+ age group**, infectious diseases and respiratory conditions become the predominant causes, reflecting the immune system decline and frailty associated with advanced old age.

This progression pattern means that the health system's elderly care burden is **not static** – it changes qualitatively as the elderly population ages. A 70-year-old and a 90-year-old present fundamentally different medical profiles, requiring different clinical expertise, different care settings, and different levels of support. The current health system, which lacks any specialised geriatric capacity, treats all elderly patients through the same generalist hospital pathways regardless of the qualitative differences in their medical needs.[^321]

### 7.12.2 The Non-Communicable Disease Cascade

The policy document warned that **"the increasing number of persons with non-communicable diseases especially amongst those reaching mid-ages could imply greater number of persons with disabilities, frailty, and mental health issues in the future."** This NCD cascade – where NCDs diagnosed in middle age (40s-50s) progress to disability, frailty, and cognitive decline in old age (60s-70s-80s) – means that the elderly care burden of the 2040s and 2050s is being determined by the NCD management (or mismanagement) of today's middle-aged population. The 30% chronic disease prevalence in the 36-64 age group (HIES 2019) represents the pipeline of future elderly patients; if their conditions are poorly managed during their working years, they will enter old age with more advanced disease and greater care needs.[^322]

### 7.12.3 The "Village" Principle and Spiritual Dimension

The policy included a distinctive guiding principle: **"It takes a village to raise a child, likewise it takes a village to enable successful and meaningful ageing."** This principle – drawn from the communal traditions of Maldivian island society – positions elderly care as a whole-of-community responsibility rather than a state or family obligation alone. The policy framework also referenced the Dahlgren and Whitehead (1991) model as incorporating **"the spiritual dimension"** – an acknowledgment that in the Maldivian Islamic context, the spiritual dimension of ageing (faith, prayer, acceptance of Allah's will) is not separate from physical and social well-being but integral to it. This integration of spiritual wellbeing into the policy framework is consistent with the findings of Nazra (2018) that Islamic faith and prayer were the most important coping mechanisms for all eight respondents.[^323]

---

---


---


## 7.13 The 2025 Policy Launch and National Action Plan on Ageing 2025-2028


### 7.13.1 Formal Policy Adoption

On 17 September 2025, the Ministry of Social and Family Development, with support from UN ESCAP, formally launched the revised National Ageing Policy at a validation workshop. This marked the culmination of the 2024 drafting process. A key innovation announced at the launch was the creation of **"Isravvehinge Naadhee"** – social centres for the elderly – a concept that had been piloted in Hulhumale' (in Hiyaa Tower 8) and was to be expanded as a nationwide model. These centres would provide health information, awareness programmes, social activities, and a gathering space for senior citizens.[^127]

### 7.13.2 National Action Plan on Ageing 2025-2028

On 9 December 2025, the government launched the **National Action Plan on Ageing 2025-2028**, translating the policy into concrete actions, timelines, and institutional responsibilities. The Action Plan was aligned with MIPAA objectives and focused on: decent work and lifelong learning; social participation; quality health services including long-term care; enabling environments; strengthened social protection; support for families and caregivers; and the development of age-friendly communities.[^128]

The UN in the Maldives highlighted the demographic urgency: 8% of the population was currently aged 60+, and the proportion aged 60 and above was expected to **triple to approximately 32% by 2050** (UN estimate). The Maldives would become an "ageing society" by 2031 (when 14% of the population is aged 65+), an "aged society" by 2044, and – according to some projections – a "super-aged society" by 2052. These thresholds, developed from international demographic classification, highlighted that the Maldives had less than a decade before crossing the first critical threshold.[^129]

---


## 7.14 The Lapsed National Plan of Action on Older Persons 2019-2023


### 7.14.1 Implementation Failure

The National Plan of Action on Older Persons 2019-2023, which operationalised the 2018 National Elderly Policy into 34 priority actions across 4 policy areas to be implemented by 10 stakeholder agencies, has **lapsed without completion**. The government's own MIPAA survey acknowledged that **"since the Action Plan was launched in November 2019, no monitoring has been done since the pandemic situation."** The COVID-19 pandemic, which struck barely four months after the plan's launch, effectively killed the implementation process. The plan was to be renewed after the development of a dedicated elderly law, which itself remains un-enacted as of 2026.[^277]

### 7.14.2 The National Committee on Healthy Ageing and Rights of Elderly

The National Committee on Healthy Ageing and Rights of Elderly, established in 2019 as the technical-level coordinating body for elderly policy, was intended to oversee implementation of the NEAP. However, the Action Plans analysis confirms that **the committee's monitoring has not occurred since the pandemic**. This means that from 2020 to at least 2024, the institutional mechanism designed to coordinate elderly policy implementation was effectively dormant – precisely during the period when the elderly population was growing, pension expenditure was rising, and the COVID-19 pandemic was exposing the vulnerability of older persons to public health emergencies.[^278]

---


## 7.15 The Policy Implementation Failure: The Government's Own Admission


### 7.15.1 Q47: The 750-Word Open-Ended Response

The survey's final substantive question (Q47) asked how the government had designed or implemented measures for the pre-ageing population. The response constitutes the government's most candid admission of policy failure:

**"Policy on older persons was gazetted in 2017, it details rights of older persons in terms of right to work, right to health, right to participate in the community and income security. However, strategies or activities has not been carried out since the endorsement of policy to ensure that these rights are attained."**

This admission – that a policy gazetted in 2017 had produced **no strategies or activities** by 2021 to ensure the rights it articulated – represents a four-year implementation void. The government further acknowledged that the 2019 action plan "was not carried out due to challenges faced during the pandemic" and that the Human Rights Commission "has highlighted the importance of having regular structured activities in this facility [Guraidhoo] several times, however, less has been achieved." The response concludes: **"Lack of day homes or communal spaces dedicated for older persons create social isolation and increases vulnerability of older persons."**[^362]

### 7.15.2 Elderly Exclusion from Policy Formulation

The survey revealed that elderly persons are systematically excluded from the policy-making process. Question 3.1.3 asked whether older persons are included in monitoring activities. The government answered "Yes" but then elaborated: **"there is an absence of such forums to engage older persons and their views during formulation of legislation, policy and action plans."** The elderly NGOs' engagement was limited to participation in Ministry-run events such as Grandparents Day, World Elder Abuse Awareness Day, and International Day of Older Persons – observance-based events rather than substantive policy consultation.[^363]

---


## 7.16 The Domestic Violence Act and the Elderly Protection Gap


### 7.16.1 Legal Protection Without Specificity

The MIPAA survey documented a critical gap in the legal framework for elder abuse: the **Domestic Violence Act 3/2012** "prohibits violence that occurs in a domestic setting, however, it doesn't state older person's rights or doesn't mention anywhere on how they will be protected." Similarly, the **Social Protection Act 2/2014** "although provide some measures to support the vulnerables in the community doesn't specify older persons and the support they might receive." The government acknowledged that "work is being carried out to enact the law on Elderly. A consultant has been hired to this and the first draft is planned to submit to Majlis in the first quarter of 2022." As of 2026, this law has still not been submitted to parliament. The hiring of a consultant and the stated timeline (Q1 2022) confirm that the law drafting process was initiated but stalled – the latest in a series of announced-but-unimplemented elderly policy commitments.[^375]

---


# Part 3: MIPAA Implementation and the 2021 Voluntary National Survey


## 7.17 MIPAA Implementation and Review


### 7.17.1 Background: The Madrid International Plan of Action on Ageing

The Madrid International Plan of Action on Ageing (MIPAA), adopted at the Second World Assembly on Ageing in 2002, is the global guiding document on population ageing. It calls for regular and systematic reviews of MIPAA implementation by Member States. The Maldives participates in the Asia-Pacific regional review process coordinated by the United Nations Economic and Social Commission for Asia and the Pacific (ESCAP).[^62]

### 7.17.2 The 2021 MIPAA Voluntary National Survey

The Maldives completed the Voluntary National Survey on MIPAA Implementation in 2021, coordinated by the Ministry of Gender, Family and Social Services. The survey was completed using "focus group meetings with key stakeholders, bottom-up approach and whole-of-government approach."[^63] The survey provides the most comprehensive official self-assessment of the Maldives' ageing policy environment.

The survey reveals both achievements and candid acknowledgments of failure across MIPAA's three priority directions:

**Priority Direction I: Older Persons and Development**
- Active participation of older persons in decision-making: "much work is required to increase their participation in other forums and committees"
- Physical activity: 70 sports council officers trained at island level in 2019 for physical activities targeting elderly
- Work and employment: "There is no law or regulation stating that older persons cannot engage in income-generating work, however, more targeted interventions are required to fully achieve this goal"
- Retirement age: Early voluntary retirement at age 55; mandatory retirement at age 65
- Elder abuse: **270 elderly abuse cases reported** to MoGFSS between 2018 and 2021, "males were more among the victims"[^64]

**Priority Direction II: Advancing Health and Well-being into Old Age**
- Substance abuse: "Government has not established successful strategies to combat substance use issue in the country. Lack of proper treatment facilities and aftercare services is noticed"
- Food security: "There is no strategies in place to ensure food security for older persons"
- Long-term care: acknowledged that work on establishing elderly homes in Male' and Addu was underway
- Health care monitoring: "Not all the health care services are monitored by the ministry"

**Priority Direction III: Ensuring Enabling and Supportive Environments**
- Housing: "Elderly population is not eligible to apply for social housing schemes"
- Transport: Free transportation for elderly above 65 on government buses and ferries in Male' and regions
- Elder abuse: Domestic Violence Act, Family Law, and Social Protection Act cover abuse; hotline 1421 available
- No measures to recognise or support the legal capacity of older persons in decision-making

### 7.17.3 The 2022 High-Level Panel Statement

At the High Level Panel Discussion of the Fourth Review and Appraisal of MIPAA in Bangkok on 29 June 2022, Minister Aishath Mohamed Didi delivered the Maldives' statement. The statement emphasised the country's commitment to elderly rights while acknowledging the challenges of being "a small island state that is now ageing rapidly."[^65] The Minister highlighted the Strategic Action Plan (SAP) 2019-2023 as the guiding policy framework and described the National Elderly Policy and National Plan of Action on Older Persons as key instruments.

The statement addressed the gender dimensions of ageing, noting the pattern where men work in the formal sector while women are in the informal sector, and emphasised the Maldives Pension Act's role in providing equal pension access. It also acknowledged that "the caregivers burden is often solely on women, which is a challenge" and highlighted the Gender Equality Action Plan (GEAP) as a response.[^66]

### 7.17.4 Assessment of MIPAA Compliance

An honest assessment of the Maldives' MIPAA compliance reveals a mixed picture. On the positive side, the country has established a National Elderly Policy, a National Plan of Action, a coordinating committee, and a universal non-contributory pension -- institutional foundations that many developing countries lack. The basic pension in particular represents a genuine commitment to elderly income security.

On the negative side, the absence of a dedicated elderly law, the lack of monitoring of the action plan, the minimal institutional care capacity, the absence of geriatric medical services, the ineligibility of elderly persons for social housing, the lack of age-friendly infrastructure, and the limited data collection on elderly welfare all represent significant gaps relative to MIPAA objectives. The government's own candid responses in the 2021 survey -- acknowledging "always" present challenges across nearly every dimension of capacity -- suggest that the institutional apparatus for elderly care remains underdeveloped relative to the demographic pressures that are already building.

---


## 7.18 The MIPAA Voluntary National Survey (2021): The Government's Self-Assessment


### 7.18.1 Survey Structure and Methodology

The Maldives' Voluntary National Survey on the Implementation of the Madrid International Plan of Action on Ageing (MIPAA) was completed by the Ministry of Gender, Family and Social Services in 2021, using focus group meetings with key stakeholders and a whole-of-government approach. The survey was structured according to MIPAA's three priority directions and was submitted to ESCAP for the Asia-Pacific Fourth Review and Appraisal in 2022. The contact office was MoGFSS, Sea Tracs Building, 7th floor, Male', with the elderly policy email elderly@gender.gov.mv. The survey represents the government's own assessment of its performance – making its documented failures and admissions particularly authoritative.[^355]

### 7.18.2 The Challenges Matrix: A Government Self-Diagnosis

The survey's Question 4 asked the government to rate nine challenges on a scale of 1 (never), 2 (sometimes), and 3 (always). The responses constitute the most candid official assessment of elderly policy barriers:

| Challenge | Rating | Government's Own Explanation |
|---|---|---|
| Lack of human resources | Sometimes | "limited agencies work towards ageing with limited dedicated and trained human resources" |
| Lack of financial resources | Sometimes | "Limited Budget dedicated for this area" |
| Lack of data | Sometimes | "Data collection is expensive with limited capacity/resources" |
| **Lack of overall political support** | **Always** | **"Other pressing issues take precedence when designing policies and dedication from government"** |
| Lack of cross-ministerial coordination | Sometimes-Always | "overarching issue of lack of coordination among government sector and private sector partners" |
| Lack of international support | Sometimes-Always | "Limited capacity to seek out international support" |
| **Lack of public awareness** | **Always** | **"Limited budget and stakeholders to work. Information on issues faced by older person is limited. lack of data in the area creates gaps in designing interventions targeted at the issues"** |
| Bottom-up approach not followed | Sometimes-Always | "Limited stakeholders working directly with elderly" |
| Misconceptions and stereotypes | Sometimes | "unknown as information is limited on this" |

The two challenges rated **"always"** – lack of political support and lack of public awareness – are structurally interconnected: without political support, resources for awareness are not allocated; without public awareness, political pressure to prioritise ageing is absent. This creates a self-reinforcing cycle of neglect that the government itself acknowledges but has not broken.[^356]

### 7.18.3 The Q4.10 Narrative: The Government's Own Words

The survey's open-ended response to Question 4.10 provides the most comprehensive official diagnosis of the elderly care crisis, in the government's own words:

**"In urban setting limited older people are visible in the community but in small island communities more people gather in parks and cafés. With any kind of health issues, even in small communities elderly stays home with low quality of life in all domains. Limited outreach services, home visits, nursing and social participation designed for older persons. Especially in urban settings such as Male' there is absence of community spaces and activities to ensure social interaction and participation of older persons in the community. No specific law targeting the elderly and their rights, and limited data on the situation of elderly which makes it difficult for policy makers to get an in-depth perception of their situation, moreover geographical challenges in data collection also makes it cumbersome. In addition to this as there is also lack of technical level human resource capacity in Maldives with regard to aging and age related matters making."**

This passage – authored by the Ministry responsible for elderly welfare – acknowledges: (a) elderly invisibility in urban settings; (b) home confinement with "low quality of life in all domains" even on outer islands; (c) limited outreach, home visits, and nursing; (d) absence of community spaces in Male'; (e) no specific law; (f) limited data; (g) geographical barriers to data collection; and (h) lack of technical human resource capacity. Every major finding documented by the academic studies (Nazra 2018, Azeez et al. 2020, Jaufar & Ibrahim 2025) and by this chapter is confirmed in the government's own assessment.[^357]

---


## 7.19 The MIPAA Survey: Remaining Government Self-Disclosures


The 2021 MIPAA Voluntary National Survey contained several responses that, while individually brief, collectively reveal the breadth of policy gaps the government itself acknowledged. These items, spanning work and employment recognition, health system participation, data infrastructure, legal capacity, and international statistical engagement, are documented below in full.

### 7.19.1 Nationwide Recognition Awards for Long-Service Workers (Q10.4)

When asked whether the government recognised the benefits of increased work experience with age in the labour market, the survey responded affirmatively and disclosed that **"In 2021, government of Maldives has started giving nationwide recognition for those who has completed a certain time in working in government and private sector. They are given recognition and encouragement award."** This initiative – recognising long-service workers through formal awards – represents the government's first explicit acknowledgement that older workers' experience has value. However, the response did not specify the eligibility criteria (how many years of service), the number of awards given, the value of the recognition (monetary or ceremonial), or whether the programme has continued beyond 2021. The initiative addresses the symbolic dimension of ageism in the workplace (recognising that older workers have contributed) but does not address the structural barriers to continued employment after retirement age.[^378]

### 7.19.2 Ranveyla as Anti-Ageism Workplace Intervention (Q10.5.2)

On whether the government had promoted age-friendly workplaces through policies and training on age inclusion or unconscious bias, the survey answered "Yes" but qualified: **"Through the 'Ranveyla' awareness program, sessions for youth are conducted with the focus on eliminating these unconscious biases and age-related discrimination. However there is no such policy specifically targeting work places."** This response reveals two things simultaneously: (a) the Ranveyla campaign has been repurposed as an anti-ageism educational tool, targeting young people's unconscious biases about elderly persons; and (b) there is no workplace-specific anti-age-discrimination policy. The response acknowledges a disconnect: youth awareness sessions may reduce social ageism, but without workplace-specific policies, age discrimination in hiring, retention, and promotion remains unaddressed. No legislation prohibits age discrimination in employment in the Maldives, and no policy requires reasonable accommodation for older workers.[^379]

### 7.19.3 Elderly Exclusion from Health Services Development (Q18.3)

Perhaps the most significant health-sector admission in the entire survey was the response to Question 18.3, which asked whether older persons were involved in the development and strengthening of health-care services. The government answered with a qualified "Yes" but elaborated: **"No data or resources suggesting that older persons are involved in the development and strengthening of health-care services."** This response – simultaneously answering "Yes" while providing evidence of "No" – reveals either a misunderstanding of the question or an attempt to present the situation more favourably than the evidence supports. The practical effect is clear: the approximately 43,000 elderly Maldivians who are the primary users of the health system have no documented mechanism for participating in its design, evaluation, or improvement. Health services are designed *for* them, not *with* them.[^380]

### 7.19.4 Healthcare Act Provisions on Age Discrimination (Q22)

When asked whether the government had undertaken actions to record and prevent age-based discrimination in access to health services, including access to providers, medicines, and preventive measures, the survey answered "Yes" and stated: **"Healthcare act provide ways to record and prevent discrimination."** This terse response refers to the Healthcare Act's general anti-discrimination provisions, which are not elderly-specific but apply to all forms of discrimination in health service access. No data was provided on whether any age-discrimination complaints had been recorded under these provisions, how many had been investigated, or what remedies were available. The existence of a general legal provision does not demonstrate that it is operationally applied to protect elderly persons from age-based rationing, delayed treatment, or differential access to specialist services.[^381]

### 7.19.5 The Absence of Legal Capacity and Supported Decision-Making Measures (Q33)

The survey's response to Question 33 – whether the government had undertaken measures to recognise and support the exercise of legal capacity of older persons, such as supported decision-making – was unequivocally **"No."** No elaboration was provided. This is a significant legal gap. Supported decision-making frameworks, which preserve the autonomy of older persons with declining cognitive capacity while providing assistance in making decisions, are considered international best practice under the Convention on the Rights of Persons with Disabilities (CRPD), to which the Maldives is a party. The absence of any such measures means that elderly persons experiencing cognitive decline face a binary choice: full legal capacity (even when they may need assistance) or full substituted decision-making through informal family arrangements that have no legal standing or oversight. There is no guardianship law specifically designed for elderly persons, no advance directive framework allowing elderly persons to specify their wishes for future care, and no legal mechanism for supported (as opposed to substituted) decision-making.[^382]

### 7.19.6 The MPAO Database on Population Aged 65+ (Q37)

In response to whether the government had undertaken data collection efforts with age disaggregation beyond 50+ or 60+, the survey disclosed that **"Household Income and Expenditure Survey (HIES) and most of the national surveys given data disaggregation by age. Pension administration Maldives also has a database with segregated data on population above 65."** This confirms that the Maldives Pension Administration Office (MPAO) maintains a database with age-disaggregated data on the population aged 65 and above – a potentially rich data source for elderly policy analysis that appears to be underutilised.
