The MPAO database, which tracks every basic pension beneficiary by name, age, sex, and island of residence, could provide island-level demographic profiles of the elderly population more current than census data. However, no evidence was found that this data has been systematically analysed for policy purposes or made available to researchers. The response also confirmed that HIES 2019 data is age-disaggregated, enabling elderly-specific economic analysis.[^383]

### 7.19.7 Non-Participation in the Titchfield Group on Ageing Statistics (Q40)

The survey confirmed that the Maldives is **not** active in the Titchfield City Group on Ageing-related Statistics and Age-disaggregated Data. The response was simply: **"None."** The Titchfield Group, established by United Nations Statistical Commission resolution 49/118 in 2018, was created to contribute to identifying gaps and establishing international standards and methods for compiling statistics on ageing and age-disaggregated data across the life course. Non-participation means the Maldives is not contributing to or benefiting from the international development of ageing-specific statistical standards. For a country undergoing one of the fastest demographic transitions in Asia, this absence from the primary international forum for ageing statistics represents a missed opportunity to both contribute its SIDS-specific experience and access methodological guidance for its own data collection efforts.[^384]

---


## 7.20 Pension Architecture: The MIPAA Survey's Detailed Self-Description


### 7.20.1 The Social Protection Floor as Described by the Government (Q13)

The MIPAA survey's response to Question 13 provided the government's own comprehensive description of the social protection architecture as it relates to income security for older persons. The response identified the following instruments:

**National Social Protection Agency (NSPA)** implements programmes under the Social Protection Act, including: disability allowance, single parent allowance, foster parent allowance, and staple food assistance programme – all delivered as monthly cash transfers. The NSPA's mandate is described as "non-contributory" to the national health care scheme (Aasandha), while the pension system combines both contributory and non-contributory elements.[^385]

**Pension Income Support** for persons above 65 years of age is provided by the Maldives Pension Administration Office (MPAO). The survey's response to Q13.2 confirmed that the **contributory Retirement Pension Scheme** is "applicable to all citizens above the age of 65 years." Q13.3 confirmed that the **non-contributory Old-Age Basic Pension**, established under Maldives Pension Act 8/2009, provides "everyone above 65 will receive pension. At present the amount provided is MRF 5000 monthly."[^385]

### 7.20.2 The Absence of Targeted Income Support and the Unemployment Insurance Plan (Q13.4-13.8)

Several sub-questions revealed significant gaps in age-specific income support:

- **Q13.4 (Pension schemes for informal sector):** "No" – there are no specific pension schemes for the informal sector, though "all citizens are eligible under the pension irrespective of whether they work in private or government sector." This means informal sector workers (89.9% of the elderly workforce) are eligible for the Basic Pension but accumulate no contributory pension savings.
- **Q13.5 (Other income-support schemes for older persons):** "No" – no data on any additional income support targeted at older persons beyond the pension.
- **Q13.6 (Income-support schemes focused on older women):** "No" – despite the documented economic vulnerability of elderly women (99.1% informal sector employment, negligible MRPS accumulation), no gender-specific income support exists.
- **Q13.7 (Financial inclusion/literacy):** "Yes" – through the Ranveyla campaign, MPAO provides information "related to the basic pension scheme and financial literacy." This confirms MPAO's active role in the Ranveyla programme as a financial literacy delivery partner.
- **Q13.8 (Other measures):** "No."

The survey also noted that **"The government is also working on implementing an unemployment insurance program through Ministry of Economic Development."** This planned programme, while not specifically targeting the elderly, could benefit older workers who face involuntary unemployment before reaching the pension eligibility age of 65. No further details on the programme's design or timeline were provided.[^386]

---


## 7.21 Free Transport Entitlement (Q26.3)


The MIPAA survey provided the government's formal confirmation of the free transport entitlement for elderly persons: **"The government offers free transportation for elderly (above 65 years of age) in the Male' region and also in regions were the government transport services such as buses and ferries are available."** This confirms that the free transport benefit is: (a) limited to persons aged 65 and above; (b) applicable in the Male' region; (c) available in other regions only where government-operated transport services exist; and (d) limited to buses and ferries – excluding private transport (taxis, speedboats) and non-government-operated ferry services. The practical implication is that elderly persons on islands without government bus or ferry service – which includes the majority of the country's 187 inhabited islands – do not benefit from this entitlement. The free transport policy addresses a real need (the MIPAA survey separately acknowledged that transport and accommodation costs are not covered by Aasandha for routine health access) but its geographic limitation means it primarily benefits elderly residents of Male', Addu, and the few other areas with government bus/ferry routes.[^387]

---


## 7.22 Social Worker Allocation to Vulnerable Elderly (Q30.2)


The survey's response to Question 30.2 – whether the government had put in place services for victims of elder abuse, neglect, and violence – stated: **"Yes as per the ministry's mandate social protection will be provided to all vulnerable elderly people and a social worker will be allocated to manage the case and provide the immediate social protection."** The use of "will be" rather than "is" is notable – it suggests an aspirational mandate rather than current operational reality. The response describes a model where individual social workers are assigned to individual vulnerable elderly cases, a case-management approach that presupposes a sufficient number of trained social workers to handle the caseload. Given that the FCSC network (the primary decentralised social service delivery mechanism) operates with minimal staff and limited budgets, and given that between 2018 and 2021 alone 270 elderly abuse cases were reported to MoGFSS, the capacity to allocate a dedicated social worker to each vulnerable elderly person remains constrained. The response did not indicate how many social workers are currently assigned to elderly cases, what training they receive in elder-specific issues, or what "immediate social protection" consists of in operational terms.[^388]

---


## 7.23 The Addu Elderly Facility: MIPAA Survey Detail


### 7.23.1 ADB Day Care and Government Residential Components

The MIPAA survey provided the most detailed contemporary description of the planned Addu facility: the Ministry "has initiated the establishment of elderly homes in 2 regions (Male' and Addu City) of Maldives. Currently work on establishing an elderly residential home along with a day care facility is underway in the southern region of Maldives. **The elderly day care facility is partially funded by the Asian Development Bank and the residential facility is funded by the Government.** The land area for Addu Elderly Home has been confirmed, the home will include a residential facility and a day care facility. The main aim of this day care component is to **ease the caregiver burden which is mostly on women**. This home is funded by the Government of Maldives. **This home is planned to open in 2023.**"

The 2023 target opening was not met. As of the Muizzu administration's May 2024 re-announcement of the Addu facility (now named 'Isravvehinge Hiyaavehi'), no opening date has been specified. The project has been announced under two different administrations (Solih and Muizzu) without being completed – spanning at least five years of planning since the MIPAA survey documentation.[^376]

---


## 7.24 Aged Care Maldives and G-Care: Operational Detail from MIPAA


### 7.24.1 Service Descriptions

The MIPAA survey provided the government's own characterisation of the three active elderly NGOs:

**Aged Care Maldives:** "a nonprofit organization that focuses on the needs of the senior populace and their families. Aged Care Maldives aims to promote healthy aging, encourage elders to be contributing members of the community and to assist those in need to maintain their individuality." The Q48 response adds: Aged Care "facilitate nursing home care for a fee."

**Dhivehi Israhvehinge Jamiyya (Maldives Senior Citizens Association):** "A non-profit organization working to create awareness on issues related to aging and elderly rights."

**G-Care Maldives:** "Nonprofit organization working for elderly rights and well-being. G care has an active group of members who are keen on teaching and sharing different skills and activities that elderly people can do at home. Before the pandemic G-Care was also actively providing transportation services for elderly people as well." The Q48 response adds: G-Care "provides voluntary services such as regular contacts over phone, support with shopping and creates social gathering opportunities for older persons."[^377]

---

---


---


# Part 4: Pension System and Social Protection


## 7.25 Elderly Allowance (Senior Citizen Pension)


### 7.25.1 The Old-Age Basic Pension

The centrepiece of Maldivian elderly social protection is the Old-Age Basic Pension (BP), established under the Maldives Pension Act (8/2009) and launched in 2010 as part of a major pension reform. The Basic Pension is a universal, non-contributory pension administered by the Maldives Pension Administration Office (MPAO) and funded entirely by the government.[^22]

**Eligibility**: Every Maldivian citizen aged 65 or older is eligible for the basic pension, regardless of income, employment history, or geographic location. The only condition is that the recipient must not already be receiving an equivalent pension amount from another source (specifically, the Maldives Retirement Pension Scheme). The pension is truly universal in its application -- it is not means-tested, not targeted to particular occupational groups, and not conditional on prior contributions.[^23]

**Amount**: The Basic Pension was initially set at MVR 2,300 per month (approximately US$150) when launched in 2010. It was subsequently increased to **MVR 5,000 per month** (approximately US$324), where it remains as of the most recent data. The amount is flat-rate -- every eligible person receives the same amount regardless of their prior earnings or other income sources.[^24]

**Deduction Mechanism**: For persons who also receive benefits from the contributory Maldives Retirement Pension Scheme (MRPS), the Basic Pension is reduced by the amount of the MRPS benefit. In other words, if a retiree receives MVR 3,000 per month from MRPS, their Basic Pension is reduced to MVR 2,000, so the total from both sources remains MVR 5,000. If the MRPS benefit exceeds MVR 5,000, the Basic Pension is not paid. This "top-up" design ensures that every elderly Maldivian receives at least MVR 5,000 per month from the pension system, while preventing double-dipping that would make the system fiscally unsustainable.[^25]

**Coverage and Expenditure**: The Basic Pension achieves near-universal coverage of the eligible population. Given that Maldives has approximately 521,000 people and approximately 5% are aged 65+, the scheme covers roughly 26,000 beneficiaries. The fiscal cost is substantial for a small island developing state -- at MVR 5,000 per month per beneficiary, the annual expenditure exceeds MVR 1.5 billion (approximately US$97 million). As the elderly population grows, this fiscal commitment will increase significantly, raising questions about long-term sustainability.[^26]

### 7.25.2 The Maldives Retirement Pension Scheme (MRPS)

Alongside the non-contributory Basic Pension, the Maldives Pension Act also established the Maldives Retirement Pension Scheme, a mandatory defined-contribution scheme for formal sector workers. Under MRPS, both employees and employers contribute a percentage of salary to individual pension accounts managed by MPAO. Upon reaching retirement age (65), the accumulated savings are converted into a monthly pension benefit.[^27]

The MRPS is significant because it represents a shift from sole reliance on government-funded pensions towards a partially funded, contributory model. However, the scheme's effectiveness is constrained by the structure of the Maldivian labour market. Formal sector employment in the Maldives is limited; as the Ministry of Social and Family Development's 2024 presentation notes, only 20.9% of labour force participation is in formal employment, while 89.9% of employment is in the informal sector.[^28] Informal sector workers -- who constitute the vast majority of the workforce -- do not contribute to MRPS and therefore rely entirely on the Basic Pension in old age.

The gender dimension is particularly concerning. Women are disproportionately represented in informal sector work (59% of informal sector employment is female, compared to 41% male), reflecting the Maldivian pattern where men have historically dominated formal sector employment while women have been "seen as caregivers and work informal sector."[^29] As Minister Aishath Mohamed Didi stated in her 2022 MIPAA address, "This resulted women unable to save any for their future, or to apply for any governmental schemes, loans. Hence a purposeful intervention was brought in the Maldives Pension Act which has enabled equal opportunities for those who are working both in informal and formal sector, without any discrimination."[^30]

### 7.25.3 Pension Adequacy and Poverty Prevention

The Basic Pension of MVR 5,000 per month provides a floor of income security that has been credited with reducing poverty among the elderly. At approximately US$324 per month, it represents a significant income stream in the Maldivian context, though its adequacy must be assessed against the high cost of living, particularly in Male' where rents are substantial.

No pension gap exists between men and women in the basic pension -- all citizens above 65 receive the same amount regardless of gender. However, the adequacy of the pension varies enormously by location. On outer islands where housing is typically owned and cost of living is lower, MVR 5,000 per month may provide a reasonable standard of living. In Male', where rent alone can consume the majority of a low-income household's budget, the pension may be insufficient to cover basic needs without supplementary family support.

### 7.25.4 Fiscal Sustainability Concerns

The long-term fiscal sustainability of the Basic Pension is a central policy challenge in elderly welfare. As the 65+ population grows from its current approximately 26,000 to potentially over 60,000 by 2045, the annual cost of the Basic Pension could more than double in real terms. This growth will occur precisely as the working-age population that generates tax revenue begins to shrink, creating a classic fiscal squeeze.

The government has not publicly released detailed actuarial projections of the pension system's long-term liabilities. The OECD's 2025 country profile for the Maldives notes the dual structure of the pension system but does not provide long-range fiscal sustainability analysis. The absence of such analysis is itself a policy gap -- without it, policymakers cannot make informed decisions about benefit levels, contribution rates, and retirement ages.[^31]

---


## 7.26 The HelpAge International Pension Case Study (2023)


### 7.26.1 Study Design and Scope

HelpAge International, with UNFPA Asia Pacific support, published a policy-in-practice case study on the Maldives' pension system in 2023 – the most detailed international assessment of the country's elderly income security architecture. The study was developed by Emeritus Professor Lakshman Dissanayake (University of Colombo, Sri Lanka) and included key informant interviews with officials from the Maldives Pension Administration Office (MPAO) and the Maldives Bureau of Statistics (MBS).[^142]

### 7.26.2 Pre-2009 System: The Old Inequity

Before the Pension Act of 2009, elderly income security was confined to public sector employees. The pre-2009 system provided a long-service allowance calculated as 50% of basic salary after 20 years of government service and 100% after 40 years. The retirement age was 65, and a voluntary Government Provident Fund required 5% contributions from both employer and employee. This system had a strong gender bias, as women's career breaks for caregiving reduced their years of service and therefore their pension entitlements. The vast majority of Maldivians – those outside the civil service – had no access to any pension whatsoever.[^143]

### 7.26.3 Detailed Monthly Beneficiary Data

The HelpAge study provided the most granular data available on pension operations. Monthly Basic Pension beneficiary and payout data for 2020 shows the scheme's scale:

| Month | Beneficiaries | Payouts (MVR millions) |
|---|---|---|
| January 2020 | 17,828 | 84.2 |
| June 2020 | 18,115 | 85.8 |
| December 2020 | 18,341 | 87.2 |

Average monthly beneficiaries in 2020 were 18,139, with total payouts of **MVR 1.03 billion** (approximately US$67 million). The steady monthly growth in beneficiaries (from 17,828 in January to 18,341 in December – an increase of 513, or roughly 43 per month) reflects the continuous ageing of the population into the 65+ eligibility threshold.[^144]

### 7.26.4 Historical Pension Growth Trajectory

The study's historical data table (2011-2019) documented the pension system's rapid growth:

| Year | Basic Pension Beneficiaries | Basic Pension Payouts (MVR millions) | Retirement Pension Beneficiaries | MRPS Contributors |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| 2011 | 15,035 | 360 | 177 | 59,002 |
| 2014 | 16,198 | 471* | 560 | 77,456 |
| 2019 | 17,496 | 904 | 1,258 | 100,225 |

(*Senior Citizen Allowance introduced in 2014, initially providing additional top-up.)

The Basic Pension payout grew from MVR 360 million in 2011 to MVR 904 million in 2019 – a 151% increase in eight years, driven by both the growth in beneficiary numbers and the doubling of the monthly amount from MVR 2,300 to MVR 5,000 in 2014. MRPS contributors grew from 59,002 to 100,225 over the same period, and the number receiving retirement pension benefits grew from 177 to 1,258 – still a small number reflecting the scheme's youth (most contributors had not yet reached retirement age).[^145]

### 7.26.5 Life-Cycle Approach and Gender Equality

The HelpAge assessment praised the Maldives' pension system for its life-cycle approach, universal coverage, and gender neutrality. The Basic Pension and MRPS covered formal and informal sectors, applied from age 16 (for MRPS contributions) to death (for Basic Pension), and did not discriminate by sex. Social pensions were found to improve older people's access to healthcare (by helping cover out-of-pocket costs), enhance self-esteem and dignity, and function as a critical mechanism for women's economic security in old age. The study noted that the system aligned with the ILO Multi-Pillar pension model, promoting universality, non-discrimination, and sustainability.[^146]

### 7.26.6 National Transfer Accounts Analysis

The case study included a National Transfer Accounts analysis showing that public and private expenditure on health consumption was "very high, especially in older age (even from age 45, health expenditure greater than other ages)." This finding has significant fiscal implications: as the population ages, aggregate health expenditure will increase not only because there will be more elderly persons but because each elderly person's health expenditure is disproportionately high. The pension system cannot be assessed in isolation from the health financing system – both will face simultaneous fiscal pressure from the same demographic driver.[^147]

---

---


## 7.27 The HelpAge Pension Case Study: Sustainability Recommendations


### 7.27.1 Fiscal Sustainability Recommendations

The HelpAge International (2023) pension case study, authored by Prof. Dissanayake, situated the Maldivian pension system within the broader context of social protection for older persons. The case study recommended three specific measures for ensuring pension sustainability: **economic growth** to expand the revenue base from which pensions are funded; **improved investment of pension funds** to generate higher returns on the MRPS assets (currently exceeding MVR 15 billion); and **delayed retirement ages** to extend the period of contributions and compress the payout period. These recommendations reflect standard international pension reform prescriptions but are politically sensitive in the Maldivian context, where the retirement age (65) is already higher than many regional comparators and where the pension's popularity makes any reform that could be perceived as a cut politically risky.[^300]

### 7.27.2 The National Transfer Accounts Analysis

The HelpAge case study cited National Transfer Accounts (NTA) analysis showing that health consumption expenditure is **disproportionately high from age 45 onwards** in the Maldives. This finding has significant fiscal implications: the pension system's costs are only one dimension of the elderly fiscal burden, alongside rapidly rising healthcare expenditure as the population ages. The NTA framework – which tracks the flow of economic resources across age groups through public and private transfers – demonstrates that the Maldives is already experiencing a net transfer of resources from the working-age population to the elderly population through both pension payments and healthcare expenditure, and this transfer will intensify as the elderly share of the population grows.[^301]

### 7.27.3 Pre-2009 System Description

The case study documented the pre-2009 pension system in detail: before the Pension Act, the only pension-like arrangements were a long-service allowance for government employees and the Government Provident Fund, both of which exhibited **gender bias** – women, who were concentrated in informal and domestic roles, were largely excluded from both schemes. The 2009 Act's introduction of the universal Basic Pension was specifically designed to address this gender inequity by creating a benefit accessible regardless of employment history. The case study confirmed that the Basic Pension had succeeded in this objective, noting the near-equal gender split in expenditure (MVR 638 million for women, MVR 635 million for men in 2024).[^302]

---


## 7.28 Pension System: Fiscal Trajectory and Sustainability


### 7.28.1 Current Expenditure Scale

The pension system's fiscal footprint has grown rapidly and now represents one of the largest single items in the government budget. According to The Maldives Journal (2025), total pension expenditure reached **MVR 1.8 billion in 2024**, a 7% increase from MVR 1.7 billion in 2023. Of this, the Basic Pension accounted for **MVR 1.3 billion** (a 7% increase), split almost evenly between women (MVR 638 million) and men (MVR 635 million) – confirming the gender-neutral design of the system. The retirement pension component reached **MVR 165 million** (a 13% rise from MVR 146 million in 2023), reflecting the growing number of MRPS contributors reaching retirement age.[^148]

### 7.28.2 Fiscal Projections and the 2054 Cliff

The long-term fiscal trajectory raises concerns. The Maldives Financial Review's analysis projected that the Basic Pension payout alone – assuming unchanged benefit levels and demographic trends – would reach:

| Year | Projected Basic Pension Payout (MVR billions) |
|---|---|
| 2020 | 1.06 |
| 2030 | 1.84 |
| 2040 | 2.80 |
| 2050 | 4.30 |
| 2054 | 5.13 |

This represents a nearly fivefold increase from 2020 to 2054, growing from MVR 1.06 billion to MVR 5.13 billion. These projections assume a constant benefit level of MVR 5,000 per month; any inflation adjustment would increase the projections further. The growth is driven entirely by demographics – the expansion of the 65+ population from approximately 18,000 in 2020 to potentially over 85,000 by 2054.[^149]

### 7.28.3 World Bank and IMF Warnings

International financial institutions have repeatedly flagged the sustainability of Maldivian pension expenditure. The World Bank warned that the basic pension system was "one of the most generous" in the world relative to income levels, and predicted "dire future without reform as working population shrinks." World Bank Lead Economist Robert Palacios specifically criticised the double-pensioning problem at the Maldives Finance Forum.[^150]

The IMF's 2024 Article IV Consultation (Country Report No. 24/106) reported that a fiscal deficit was projected at 12.2% of GDP in 2024, with total debt exceeding 115% of GDP. The IMF's Fiscal Affairs Department (FAD) assisted authorities in "assessing pension system sustainability" in November 2023 – an indication that the pension system's fiscal implications had risen to the level of direct IMF engagement. The pension system must be understood within this broader fiscal context: every additional rufiyaa spent on pensions in an environment of high deficits and unsustainable debt levels carries a higher cost than it would in a fiscally healthy environment.[^151]

### 7.28.4 The IOPS Country Profile (2025)

The OECD's International Organisation of Pension Supervisors (IOPS) published its Maldives Country Profile in May 2025, providing the most detailed international assessment of the pension system's regulatory architecture. The profile confirmed that prior to 2009, only government employees were eligible for any pension, and that the Pension Act 8/2009 transformed the system from a narrow civil-service benefit to a universal entitlement. The profile noted two unique features of the Maldivian system: the **housing collateralisation scheme** (allowing MRPS balances to be used as loan collateral for housing purchases) and the **Hajj scheme** (allowing members with a Retirement Savings Account balance of MVR 336,000 or more to withdraw funds for the Islamic pilgrimage).[^152]

### 7.28.5 Intergenerational Risk

The Maldives Policy Think Tank's November 2025 analysis, "Borrowing From Tomorrow: The Maldives Budget 2026, Demographic Realities, and Intergenerational Risk," provided the most sophisticated domestic analysis of pension fiscal dynamics. The analysis warned that the labour force would "gradually contract after 2038," and that using pension assets for short-term fiscal management risked "undermining pension credibility." The central argument was that younger contributors were "effectively subsidising present consumption" – a mechanism of intergenerational borrowing in which current retirees' benefits were being paid not from their own accumulated contributions but from the contributions and tax payments of working-age citizens who themselves would face an even larger elderly dependency burden when they retired.[^153]

This intergenerational dynamic creates a political economy trap: current retirees and near-retirees have strong incentives to resist reform (since they benefit from the current generosity), while younger workers who will bear the fiscal consequences have less political voice. The longer reform is delayed, the more abrupt the eventual adjustment will need to be.

---


## 7.29 The Double-Pensioning Problem


### 7.29.1 Institutional Pension Schemes Outside the General System

One of the most significant structural problems in the Maldivian pension system is the proliferation of institutional pension schemes that operate outside – and on top of – the general pension framework. According to Corporate Maldives (2024), approximately **15 institutions** operate their own pension schemes in addition to the MRPS, creating widespread "double-pensioning" where employees receive benefits from both their institutional scheme and the general pension system.[^154]

The institutions receiving preferential pension benefits include the Judicial Service Commission, Police Service, President's Office, Anti-Corruption Commission, Civil Service Commission, Elections Commission, Maldives Customs Service, Maldives Correctional Service, Maldives National University (MNU), and the Islamic University of Maldives (IUM). Nine government institutions have established their own defined-benefit pension schemes that guarantee benefits irrespective of contributions – unlike the MRPS's defined-contribution model where benefits depend on accumulated savings.[^155]

### 7.29.2 Fiscal and Equity Implications

The double-pensioning system creates both fiscal unsustainability and profound inequity. The fiscal burden of maintaining parallel pension schemes for privileged institutions diverts resources that could be used to strengthen the universal Basic Pension or fund elderly care services. The equity problem is equally serious: public sector employees in these institutions receive pension benefits far in excess of what private sector workers or informal sector workers can expect, despite all groups contributing to the tax base that funds the Basic Pension.

The Maldives Financial Review's "Pensions Burden" analysis detailed how several state institutions had established parallel pension schemes on top of MRPS, and the MMA (Maldives Monetary Authority) had warned in 2015 that the "sudden implementation of large-scale social protection programs" had occurred when "not enough fiscal space" existed, creating a "large and potentially unsustainable burden."[^156]

---


## 7.30 The Pension as Poverty Reduction and Family Economic Anchor


### 7.30.1 Case Evidence

The comprehensive lit review document provides a case study that illustrates the pension's impact: "Consider an elderly couple on a small island in Haa Alif atoll. Before 2009, they relied on irregular remittances from a son in Male' and the fish the husband could catch despite his age. They lived frugally and often had to ask neighbours for help. After the Pension Act, by 2010 the husband (over 65) started receiving Rf 2,000 as basic pension, and by 2014 it increased to Rf 5,000. Suddenly, the couple had a secure monthly income." The case study noted that surveys showed the basic pension "dramatically reduced elderly poverty" and that the World Bank had called the scheme "one of the most generous" in the world.[^210]

### 7.30.2 The Pension as Family Income Source

On outer islands, the elderly pension is often the most reliable income source in the household, transforming the economic position of the elderly from dependents to contributors. The same case study observed: "That couple's story is multiplied by thousands of households where elders now contribute to family income through their pension, rather than being seen as a burden. This has social implications too: it elevates the status of the elderly in families (they control a resource) and provides them dignity." However, the study also noted the sustainability concern: "as more people age into the pension, the fiscal cost rises. Maldives has kept it universal, resisting means-testing so far. Any such move is sensitive."[^211]

### 7.30.3 Disability Among the Elderly

Census 2022 data analysed in the comprehensive social policy report found that "among elderly (65+) it spikes to over 20% having some disability, while among children (5-17) it's 4%." The 2024 National Ageing Policy cited an even higher figure: 45% of elderly have "some form of disability." These data illustrate the intersection of ageing and disability – as the population ages, the demand for disability services, assistive devices, and accessibility accommodations will increase proportionally, creating compounding fiscal pressures on both the pension system and the disability allowance system.[^212]

---


## 7.31 The Pension's Economic Power: Poverty Reduction and Adequacy


### 7.31.1 Pension Generosity in Comparative Perspective

The "Ageing Ungracefully" analysis provided a critical data point on pension adequacy: according to the Maldives Pension Administration Office, the Basic Pension benefits are **222 percent over the national poverty line and 338 percent higher than the international poverty line**. This makes the Maldivian Basic Pension one of the most generous social pensions globally in terms of its purchasing power relative to the poverty threshold. The pension covers approximately **95 percent** of the elderly population – confirming near-universal coverage. The combination of generous benefit levels and near-universal coverage represents an extraordinary fiscal commitment to elderly income security that has few parallels among upper-middle-income countries.[^245]

### 7.31.2 The Adequacy Paradox: Living Costs vs. Poverty Lines

However, the "Ageing Ungracefully" analysis also exposed the paradox of pension adequacy: a pension that is 222% above the poverty line may still be insufficient for a dignified life in Male', where living costs far exceed the national poverty threshold. The article posed a telling hypothetical: "For example, a 40-year-old who has migrated to Male' for education, health or employment opportunities, contributes to the pension scheme and has a stable job. He, however, lives in rented accommodation and does not earn enough to purchase private housing, nor is fortunate enough to be selected in the social housing schemes announced by the government. When this individual turns 65 and has to retire, where do they live? Is the payout from the pension scheme enough to sustain the individual's lifestyle then, with rent and other overheads?" The answer, implicitly, is no – the MVR 5,000 per month pension cannot sustain a rental lifestyle in Male', particularly when the elderly are excluded from social housing eligibility.[^246]

---


## 7.32 Senior Citizen Allowance: Complete Beneficiary Time Series


### 7.32.1 Growth Trajectory 2009-2024

The Statistics Compendium and social protection analysis together provide the most complete time series of Basic Pension beneficiary numbers and expenditure:

| Year | Beneficiaries | Monthly Rate (MVR) | Annual Expenditure (MVR) |
|---|---|---|---|
| 2009 | 13,421 | 2,300 | 310.9 million |
| 2010 | 14,717 | 2,300 | – |
| 2014 | – | 5,000 (increased) | – |
| 2020 | ~18,000 | 5,000 | ~1.03 billion |
| 2024 | ~20,000+ (est.) | 5,000 | ~1.3 billion |

The expenditure trajectory – from MVR 310.9 million in 2009 to approximately MVR 1.3 billion in 2024 – represents a more than fourfold increase in fifteen years, driven by three factors: the doubling of the benefit level from MVR 2,300 to MVR 5,000 (in 2014), population ageing expanding the beneficiary pool from 13,421 to over 20,000, and the near-universal coverage rate (95% of eligible population).[^283]

---


## 7.33 Updated Pension System Data (2024-2025)


### 7.33.1 Fund Size and Expenditure Update

As of Q1 2025, the Maldives Pension Administration Office manages accumulated assets equivalent to **US$1.68 billion** (exceeding the previously reported MVR 15 billion). The 2024 pension expenditure breakdown provides updated gender-disaggregated data:

| Component | 2024 (MVR) | 2023 (MVR) | Change |
|---|---|---|---|
| **Basic Pension** | 1.3 billion | 1.2 billion | +7% |
| – Women | 638 million | – | – |
| – Men | 635 million | – | – |
| **Retirement Pension** | 165 million | 146 million | +13% |
| – Men | 126 million | – | – |
| – Women | 38 million | – | – |
| **Other Pension Schemes** | 364 million | 358 million | +2% |
| **Total** | **1.8 billion** | **1.7 billion** | **+7%** |

The retirement pension gender gap is striking: men received **MVR 126 million** versus women's **MVR 38 million** – a ratio of more than 3:1. This disparity reflects the structural gender inequality in formal sector employment: since women constitute 99.1% of informal sector workers, their MRPS accumulations are minimal, and their retirement pension payouts are correspondingly small. The Basic Pension, by contrast, shows near-perfect gender equity (MVR 638M vs MVR 635M), confirming its function as a gender-neutral floor that compensates for the gendered inequality of the contributory system.[^330]

### 7.33.2 The Double-Pensioning Scale

Updated data from Corporate Maldives (2024) reveals that the approximately 15 institutions operating outside the general pension fund allocate around **MVR 250 million annually** in parallel pension payments. The MPAO fund size has grown to **over MVR 20 billion** in assets. World Bank Lead Economist Robert Palacios, who designed the original 2009 system, criticised double pensions at the Maldives Finance Forum, advocating for "halting exclusive institutional pensions and realigning the system with 2009 principles." President Muizzu pledged campaign promises to enable pension fund investments in private and tourism sectors to boost returns, though no new laws had passed nearly a year into his tenure despite draft reforms reportedly being in progress.[^331]

### 7.33.3 Basic Pension Application Process: Detailed Procedure

The MPAO website provides the complete application procedure, which is notably simple by design:

1. Citizens may submit a 'Pension Applicant's Information Form' **6 months before reaching age 65**
2. Forms are available from the MPAO website (pension.gov.mv), the Pension Office reception, and island/atoll council offices
3. **No supporting documents are required** – the application form alone suffices, with identity verified through the national ID system
4. The applicant must have an active bank account in their name at a Maldivian bank
5. If the applicant is already over 65 at the time of application, pension deposits commence from the month of receipt – **back payments are not allowed** for the elapsed period
6. The Basic Pension amount since March 2019 is MVR 5,000, with deductions for those receiving MRPS or other state-funded pensions

The simplicity of the application process (no means test, no supporting documents, accessible from island council offices) is a deliberate design choice that maximises uptake among elderly populations with limited literacy and bureaucratic capacity. However, the no-back-payment rule means that elderly persons who are unaware of the pension, who lack a bank account, or who face practical barriers to submitting the form lose entitlement for every month of delay – a hidden exclusion mechanism that disproportionately affects the most isolated and vulnerable elderly.[^332]

### 7.33.4 Public Complaints and Reform Pressures

Corporate Maldives documented growing public dissatisfaction with the pension system: the MVR 5,000 basic pension is increasingly deemed **"insufficient and unfairly calculated"**; early withdrawal conditions are considered restrictive; and there is frustration at limited fund utility. The Maldivian National Party introduced a parliamentary motion to treat old-age and retirement pensions as separate entitlements (which would eliminate the deduction mechanism), but parliament dismissed the motion with minimal support for systemic revision. The political economy of pension reform remains constrained: current beneficiaries resist any changes that could reduce their benefits, while younger contributors lack the political organisation to advocate for long-term sustainability reforms.[^333]

---


## 7.34 Updated Pension Data: 2024-2025 MPAO Statistics


### 7.34.1 MRPS Fund Growth

As of Q1 2025, the total MRPS fund value reached **MVR 25.5 billion (approximately USD 1.65 billion)**, including recognition bonds and the housing fund. The net asset value (NAV), excluding these instruments, stood at **MVR 21.26 billion**. The fund grew by MVR 2.9 billion between Q1 2024 and Q1 2025 – a 12.83% annual increment. The MRPS fund now represents **24.29% of GDP** (as of December 2024), up from 6% in 2010. Total MRPS membership reached **192,057** as of March 2025, including 6,793 expatriates. The mandatory defined contribution rate remains at 14% of pensionable wage (7% employee + 7% employer).[^432]

### 7.34.2 Pension Expenditure 2024

Total pension expenditure in 2024 reached **MVR 1.8 billion** – a 7% increase from MVR 1.7 billion in 2023. The breakdown:

- **Basic Pension (OABP):** MVR 1.3 billion (72% of total pension expenditure)
  - Women: MVR 638 million
  - Men: MVR 635 million
- **Retirement Pension (MRPS):** MVR 165 million (9%), up 13% from MVR 146 million in 2023
  - Men: MVR 126 million
  - Women: MVR 38 million
- **Other Pensions:** MVR 364 million (20%)[^433]

### 7.34.3 The Retirement Pension Gender Gap

The 2023 retirement pension beneficiary data reveals a stark gender disparity: of the **1,869 retirement pension beneficiaries**, only **411 are women (22%) compared to 1,458 men (78%)**. The expenditure gap is even wider: men received MVR 126 million (77%) while women received MVR 38 million (23%). This 3.5:1 male-to-female ratio in retirement pension beneficiaries directly reflects the gender gap in formal sector employment. With 99.1% of elderly women having worked in the informal sector (and therefore not contributing to MRPS), the vast majority of elderly women rely entirely on the basic pension of MVR 5,000/month. The retirement pension system, designed as the contributory pillar, functions in practice as a benefit primarily for men who had formal sector careers. This structural gender inequality in retirement income will persist for decades, as today's working-age women continue to be disproportionately represented in informal employment.[^434]

### 7.34.4 Basic Pension Beneficiaries by Age (2023)

The age distribution of basic pension beneficiaries in 2023 provides insight into the pensioner population:

| Age Group | Beneficiaries |
|---|---|
| 65-69 | 8,296 |
| 70-74 | 5,324 |
| 75 and over | 8,403 |
| **Total** | **22,023** |

The near-equal distribution between the "younger elderly" (65-69: 8,296) and the "oldest old" (75+: 8,403) indicates that the pension system serves both the recently retired and those who have been receiving benefits for a decade or more. The 75+ group, which represents the highest-cost cohort in terms of healthcare utilisation, constitutes 38% of all basic pension beneficiaries.[^435]

---


## 7.35 The Gendered Pension Gap: CEDAW Analysis


### 7.35.1 Structural Old-Age Economic Vulnerability for Women

The CEDAW Periodic Report draft provides the most explicit articulation of the gendered pension gap. The Maldives Retirement Pension Scheme (MRPS) provides for individual account accumulation based on employment contributions. Given women's lower formal sector participation and shorter careers due to caregiving interruptions, **women's pension accumulations are structurally lower than men's, creating old-age economic vulnerability**. A non-contributory pension (the Basic Pension) exists for those without formal employment records, but the MVR 5,000 monthly floor is the maximum that most women can expect – while men with formal sector careers may receive substantially more through their MRPS accumulations on top of the Basic Pension. The CEDAW report notes that women constitute the majority of Basic Pension recipients due to longer life expectancy, but this higher participation rate masks the lower total retirement income that women receive compared to men.[^280]

### 7.35.2 The GEAP and Elderly Women

The Gender Equality Action Plan (GEAP) 2022-2026 includes provisions for "supporting programmes for economic empowerment of vulnerable women" explicitly including elderly women alongside persons with disabilities, single mothers, and GBV victims. This cross-referencing between gender policy and elderly welfare is significant because it represents recognition that elderly women face compounded disadvantage – the intersection of age, gender, and economic vulnerability – that requires coordinated attention from both the ageing and gender policy frameworks.[^281]

---


## 7.36 Social Protection Architecture and Elderly Care


### 7.36.1 The Three-Tier Social Protection Floor

The Maldivian social protection system, as conceptualised under the National Social Protection Framework (NSPF) 2022-2026, operates on a three-tier architecture with direct relevance to elderly welfare. At the base is a **social floor** guaranteeing access to essential health services, primary and lower-secondary education, and basic income support for vulnerable groups – of which the Basic Pension is the primary elderly component. The second tier consists of **mandatory social insurance**: universal health insurance (Aasandha) and the defined-contribution Maldives Retirement Pension Scheme (MRPS). The third tier encompasses **voluntary insurance**. This architecture means that elderly persons are covered by all three tiers simultaneously: the Basic Pension (social floor), Aasandha and MRPS income (mandatory insurance), and any private insurance they may hold. The lifecycle protection principle underlying this framework – "ensuring that social protection programs cover risks at every stage of life, from childhood nutrition... to old-age pensions and disability assistance" – positions elderly welfare as the culminating stage of a lifelong social protection journey.[^264]

### 7.36.2 Elderly as the Largest Single Fiscal Commitment

Social protection expenditure data reveals that elderly assistance constituted **42 per cent of total social protection expenditure in 2009** – the largest single programme category. The Old-Age Basic Pension alone accounted for MVR 310.9 million, followed by the Redundancy and Early Retirement programme at MVR 103.4 million. By contrast, institutional care for the mentally ill and elderly at Guraidhoo cost MVR 21.8 million (2008), declining to MVR 19.5 million (2010) – covering approximately 150-160 persons. These figures confirm that the Maldivian social protection system was, from its inception, elderly-centric in fiscal terms. By 2024, with pension expenditure exceeding MVR 1.8 billion, this elderly dominance of social protection spending has only intensified.[^265]

### 7.36.3 Means-Testing Implementation Risk

The announced means-testing reform of the Basic Pension (late 2024, for April 2025 implementation) carries a specific risk identified in the social protection analysis: the potential exclusion of **vulnerable seniors who lack documentation**. Income verification in a country where 89.9% of employment is informal – and where elderly persons may have never had formal employment records, tax returns, or bank accounts documenting their income – creates a practical barrier. Elderly persons on outer islands who have lived their entire lives outside the formal economy may be unable to demonstrate that they fall below the income threshold, potentially losing pension eligibility despite being among the poorest. This risk is particularly acute for elderly women, who are disproportionately represented in informal employment (99.1% of female employment is informal) and may lack the documentation or digital literacy to navigate means-testing verification processes.[^266]

---


## 7.37 The MMA Warning: Central Bank Assessment of Fiscal Sustainability


### 7.37.1 The 2015 Fiscal Costs Analysis

The Maldives Monetary Authority (MMA), the country's central bank, issued a policy note in 2015 providing the first macroeconomic assessment of social protection fiscal sustainability. The MMA stated that the **"sudden implementation of large-scale social protection programs"** and subsidy expansion had occurred when there was **"not enough fiscal space,"** creating **"a large and potentially unsustainable burden on public finances."** The analysis detailed rising costs across key programmes, with the pension system identified as one of the primary drivers of fiscal pressure. The MMA warning is significant because it came from the country's central monetary authority – an institution whose mandate is financial stability rather than social policy – and represented a formal institutional alarm about the trajectory of social protection spending, including the elderly pension.[^307]

---


## 7.38 The UNESCAP Social Protection Readiness Assessment (2023)


### 7.38.1 International Benchmarking

The 2023 UNESCAP assessment of the Maldives' readiness to implement the regional Action Plan to Strengthen Social Protection commended the Maldives for its **"strong commitment to social protection,"** highlighting the **near-universal old-age pension**, universal disability allowance, and universal health insurance as key achievements. This external validation from UNESCAP – contrasting with the IMF and World Bank's fiscal sustainability warnings – illustrates the fundamental tension in elderly policy: the pension system is simultaneously one of the Maldives' greatest social policy achievements (providing near-universal income security to the elderly) and one of its greatest fiscal risks (consuming an ever-growing share of public expenditure in a country with high debt and narrow revenue base).[^308]

---


## 7.39 The ADB Social Protection Index Assessment


### 7.39.1 System Performance (2009 Data)

The Asian Development Bank's Social Protection Index (SPI) assessment for the Maldives, published in 2012 with data from 2009, provided the first comprehensive quantitative evaluation of the Maldivian social protection system's performance. The SPI was calculated at **0.073**, meaning government spending on social protection was equivalent to only 7.3% of the amount required to lift every citizen to the poverty line. The analysis found the system was "both shallow and narrow" – the depth of protection (average benefit size) was 43.2% of per capita poverty line expenditures, while the breadth (proportion of beneficiaries reached) was 16.8%.[^208]

### 7.39.2 Regressive Distribution

Most critically for elderly policy, the ADB found the system to be **highly regressive**: the non-poor received 80% of total social protection expenditure. This regressivity was "primarily driven by the cost of pensions and provident fund schemes benefiting former civil service employees rather than the poorest populations." In the pre-2009 reform era, the pension system was a mechanism for transferring public resources to relatively privileged civil servants, while the poorest elderly – those outside the public sector – received nothing. The 2009 Pension Act's universal Basic Pension was designed specifically to address this regressivity, and by most measures it has succeeded in creating a floor that reaches all elderly regardless of employment history. However, the persistence of double-pensioning for institutional employees (see §7.21) suggests that the regressive tendencies of the pre-2009 system have not been fully eliminated.[^209]

---


# Part 5: Institutional Care and Facilities


## 7.40 Guraidhoo and Other Facilities


### 7.40.1 Home for People with Special Needs (HPSN), K. Guraidhoo

The primary state-run residential care facility for the elderly in the Maldives is the **Home for People with Special Needs (HPSN)**, located on the island of Guraidhoo in Kaafu Atoll. Originally known as the "Guraidhoo Home" and at various times referred to as the home for elderly and persons with disabilities, this facility serves as the country's only functioning long-term residential care institution for elderly persons who cannot be cared for in family settings.[^32]

The HPSN caters to a mixed population: elderly persons who are abandoned, have no family, or whose families cannot care for them; and persons with severe disabilities requiring institutional care. The facility is managed by the Ministry of Social and Family Development (formerly MoGFSS). As the MIPAA survey response notes, "Home for People with Special Needs (HPSN) caters to the needs of elderly at state care" and "houses elderly patients who are looked after by state care."[^33]

**Conditions and Challenges**: The HPSN faces significant operational challenges. Medical treatment is provided within the facility, but the MIPAA survey acknowledged that "although medical treatment is provided in HPSN, there is lack of structured program and measures to engage elderly in daily activities of living."[^34] This points to a care model focused on basic custodial care (housing, feeding, medical treatment) rather than a comprehensive approach that addresses the social, psychological, and recreational needs of residents.

The facility's location on Guraidhoo, a relatively remote island, means that residents are physically separated from their home communities, families, and the broader social networks that are fundamental to well-being in Maldivian culture. For elderly residents with intact but unable families, the distance makes regular family visits difficult and expensive, compounding the isolation inherent in institutional placement.

### 7.40.2 Planned Elderly Homes in Male' and Addu City

Recognising the inadequacy of a single residential facility for the entire nation, the government has been working to establish elderly homes in two additional locations: the Greater Male' Region and Addu City (the southernmost population centre).[^35]

**Addu City Elderly Home**: The most advanced of these projects, the Addu Elderly Home was planned as both a residential facility and a day care facility. According to the 2021 MIPAA survey, the elderly day care facility is partially funded by the Asian Development Bank, while the residential facility is funded by the government. The land area for the Addu facility has been confirmed, and the home was planned to open in 2023. The day care component is specifically designed to ease the caregiver burden "which is mostly on women," reflecting the gendered nature of eldercare in the Maldives.[^36]

**Male' Region Elderly Home**: The Elderly Home planned for the Male' region was initially intended for Hulhumale', but the allocated land "didn't meet the requirements" and the project was "at a halt" as of 2021.[^37] This stalling reflects the intense competition for land in the Male' region, where residential, commercial, and institutional uses compete for extremely limited space on densely populated islands.

### 7.40.3 The Institutional Care Gap

The reality is that the Maldives has a profound deficit in institutional care capacity. With only one functioning facility (HPSN on Guraidhoo), the country cannot provide residential care to the growing number of elderly persons who require it. The planned facilities in Addu and Male', even if completed, would add limited capacity relative to the projected growth in the elderly population.

This gap is compounded by the geographic dispersion of the population across approximately 187 inhabited islands. Even if residential facilities existed in Male' and Addu, elderly persons on islands in the central and northern atolls would face significant barriers to accessing them, including the cost of inter-island transport, separation from home communities, and the cultural reluctance to send elderly family members away.

The absence of any regulatory framework for private-sector elderly care facilities means that there is no quality assurance mechanism for any non-governmental residential care that may emerge. No licensing standards, staffing ratios, or care quality benchmarks have been established for elderly care facilities, public or private.

---


## 7.41 Guraidhoo Facility: Physical Conditions, Upgrades, and Reform


### 7.41.1 Japan Government Grant (2020)

The physical condition of the Home for People with Special Needs (HPSN) on K. Guraidhoo attracted international attention and assistance. In January 2020, the Government of Japan granted **USD 86,961** for upgrading the facility, including the installation of a fully functional industrial kitchen. The grant was part of Japan's Grassroots Human Security Projects programme, reflecting the facility's severe infrastructure deficiencies. The Japanese Embassy noted concerns over the increasing number of elderly residents and the lack of adequate space – problems that a kitchen upgrade alone could not solve but that highlighted the facility's basic operational challenges.[^157]

### 7.41.2 BML Infrastructure Upgrades

The Bank of Maldives (BML) subsequently completed a separate round of infrastructure upgrades to the Guraidhoo facility, contributing to improved living conditions. These upgrades, funded through BML's corporate social responsibility programme, addressed some of the physical infrastructure deficiencies but did not resolve the fundamental structural issues: the facility's remote location, the absence of structured daily activity programmes, the mixed-population model (elderly and persons with severe disabilities housed together), and the lack of trained geriatric care staff.[^158]

### 7.41.3 Aged Care Maldives Partnership

A significant development was the Memorandum of Understanding signed between the Ministry of Gender, Family and Social Services and **Aged Care Maldives** (the NGO) to improve services for elderly residents at the Guraidhoo facility. Under this one-year project, caregivers at the HPSN received certified training, and the partnership aimed to create "a safe caring environment for elderly residents." This represented the first formal partnership between the government and a civil society organisation specifically focused on improving elderly care within the state institutional system.[^159]

### 7.41.4 Persistent Challenges

Despite these upgrades and partnerships, the fundamental challenges of the Guraidhoo facility remain. The MIPAA survey (2021) acknowledged that "although medical treatment is provided in HPSN, there is lack of structured program and measures to engage elderly in daily activities of living." The facility was originally designed for persons with mental illness and was repurposed to house elderly residents – a population whose needs differ significantly. The lack of structured daily activities, therapeutic programming, and social engagement opportunities means that residents receive custodial care (housing, feeding, basic medical treatment) rather than the comprehensive aged care that promotes dignity, autonomy, and quality of life.[^160]

---


## 7.42 HPSN Guraidhoo: UN Working Group on Arbitrary Detention Findings


### 7.42.1 International Scrutiny

The conditions at the Home for People with Special Needs (HPSN) on K. Guraidhoo attracted international human rights scrutiny. The **UN Working Group on Arbitrary Detention (2021)** conducted an assessment finding **25 elderly persons** resident at the facility, sometimes housed in **wards mixed with psychiatric patients**. The Human Rights Commission of the Maldives (HRCM) shared its own concerns with the Ministry of Gender, Family and Social Services regarding conditions at the facility. These findings reinforced the characterisation of Guraidhoo as an institution providing custodial rather than dignified aged care – a facility designed for psychiatric patients that has been repurposed to house elderly residents for whom no alternative exists, with co-location practices that raise fundamental questions about the rights and dignity of elderly residents.[^276]

---


## 7.43 Chronology of Elderly Care Facility Announcements: Promises and Reality


### 7.43.1 The Repeated Promise of Dedicated Elderly Homes

The history of elderly care facility development in the Maldives is characterised by a pattern of announcements followed by minimal implementation. A chronological review reveals:

**February 7, 2019:** President Ibrahim Mohamed Solih announced plans for the country's **first-ever elderly housing programme** during his inaugural parliamentary sitting, stating its purpose was "to meet the housing and care needs of senior citizens." No budget, location, or timeline was specified.[^324]

**October 7, 2019:** Minister of Gender, Family and Social Services Shidhatha Shareef stated on RaajjeTV's *Fala Surukhee* programme that elderly homes would be established **"within the coming two years"** – targeting 2021. She confirmed one facility would be in Male' City, with a second location "yet to be confirmed." The Minister acknowledged that Guraidhoo "does not house the necessary requirements or provide the most suitable environment" and that the existing conditions "cause emotional distress to elderly residents." She cited Gender Ministry statistics showing **over 70 elderly-related cases annually**. No budget was specified.[^325]

**February 17, 2023:** President Solih announced the development of **community centres for the elderly "across the entire country"** at a National Award for Public Service ceremony at Dharubaaruge. The Male' facility would be constructed at the former "Naadhee" building site, historically the People's Majlis. The centres would provide spaces for seniors to meet and socialise, offering "social support services as well as entertainment and wellness activities." No specific completion dates or budget were provided.[^326]

**February 17, 2024:** President Dr Mohamed Muizzu and First Lady Madam Sajidha Mohamed inaugurated the first **Isravvehinge Naadhee** senior citizens' centre in Male'. The facility was the first built under the Muizzu administration's initiative. By March 2025, officials from the Maldives Senior Citizens Association discussed ways to enhance its functioning and increase senior citizen participation.[^327]

**May 21, 2024:** President Muizzu announced the construction of **'Isravvehinge Hiyaavehi'** (a dedicated elderly home) in Addu City, Feydhoo, following a Cabinet decision. The facility would serve elderly individuals lacking family support, providing "quality services to nurture an environment where senior citizens in need of care can spend the rest of their lives happy and content." A novel **rewards programme** was also announced, offering points to individuals who live with or care for elderly citizens in new housing schemes. The government committed to amending laws protecting senior citizens' rights. No construction timeline or budget was specified.[^328]

### 7.43.2 The Implementation Gap

The chronology reveals a five-year pattern (2019-2024) of announcements without completion. Minister Shidhatha's 2019 promise of elderly homes "within two years" would have targeted 2021; as of 2026, no dedicated elderly residential facility has been completed. The MIPAA survey (2021) documented that the Male' elderly home "was supposed to be located in Hulhumale' initially, however, the land that was allocated for this purpose didn't meet the requirements and is at a halt." The Addu City facility remains at the announcement stage. The only tangible infrastructure outcome across three administrations (Solih 2018-2023, transition, Muizzu 2023-present) is the single Isravvehinge Naadhee community centre – a day-use social space, not a residential care facility.[^329]

---


## 7.44 Isravvehinge Naadhee: A New Model for Elderly Social Infrastructure


### 7.44.1 Concept and Design

The most significant recent innovation in Maldivian elderly care infrastructure is the **Isravvehinge Naadhee** ("Senior Citizens' Centre") concept, announced as part of the 2025 National Ageing Policy launch. The centres are designed as community-based social hubs for older persons, providing health information and awareness programmes, social activities, intergenerational events, and a dedicated gathering space for senior citizens.[^161]

### 7.44.2 Hulhumale Pilot

The first Isravvehinge Naadhee was established in **Hiyaa Tower 8, Hulhumale'**, within the social housing complex that was among the largest housing developments in the Maldives. The location was strategic: Hulhumale' has a rapidly growing population including many families who relocated from other islands, and the social housing towers concentrate a population that includes elderly persons who may be isolated in their apartments. The centre's placement within a housing tower allows elderly residents to access it without navigating Male's congested streets.[^162]

### 7.44.3 The Maldives Senior Citizens Association Engagement

The Maldives Senior Citizens Association discussed enhancements to the Isravvehinge Naadhee concept with President Muizzu, advocating for expanded programming and dedicated space. The Association highlighted the difficulty that elderly NGOs faced in securing office space – a practical constraint that limited their ability to operate and advocate. The Pension Administration Office signed an agreement to support the Association's administrative functions, reflecting a growing recognition that elderly civil society organisations required institutional support to fulfil their advocacy role.[^163]

### 7.44.4 Potential for National Rollout

The Isravvehinge Naadhee concept addresses a gap identified across multiple studies: the near-total absence of community spaces designed for elderly socialisation and activity. Nazra's (2018) respondents described being confined to apartments; the MIPAA survey noted the "absence of community spaces and activities to ensure social interaction" in Male'; and Azeez et al. (2020) documented 56.1% depression rates linked to social isolation. If the model is replicated across urban centres and adapted for outer island contexts (perhaps using existing community buildings such as the holhuashi or community centres), it could provide the physical infrastructure for the community-based ageing approach that multiple studies have recommended.[^164]

---


## 7.45 Private Sector Elderly Care: Emerging Services


### 7.45.1 ADK@Home

Beyond the three private sector services previously identified (Maldives Mobile Nursing Care, Royal Nursing Home, and Medica Hospital geriatrics), the service directory identifies **ADK@Home**, a home healthcare service operated by ADK Hospital (one of the two largest private hospitals in Male'). ADK@Home provides home-based care for elderly and post-operative patients, filling a gap left by the limited public Home Visit Programme. The emergence of private home healthcare services reflects both the unmet demand for elderly home care and the ability of some families to purchase privately what the public system cannot provide – creating a two-tier system where wealthier families can access private home care while poorer families rely on the 67-patient public programme or informal family care alone.[^286]

---

---


---


# Part 6: Home and Community-Based Care


## 7.46 Home Care and Community-Based Support


### 7.46.1 The Government Home Visit Program for Bedridden Patients

The most significant community-based elderly care initiative in the Maldives is the **Government Home Visit Program for Bedridden Patients**, launched in 2015 in collaboration between the Ministry of Gender and Family (now Ministry of Social and Family Development) and the Ministry of Health. The program represents the country's primary formal response to the long-term care needs of elderly persons who are homebound.[^38]

**Program Design**: The program targets fully bedridden, 65-years-and-above-aged patients who are either completely bedridden or have limited mobility. Families are required to register the patient through the Ministry of Gender and Families, and the home visit program is then conducted in collaboration with the Ministry of Health. In the Greater Male' region, the program operates through Dhamanaveshi and the Public Health Unit of Hulhumale Hospital, which are the two main urban primary health care centres. In the atolls, the program is implemented through Health Centres and Island Council Offices.[^39]

**Services Provided**: Routine home visits occur twice a week and include the following services:[^40]

- General consultation and examination
- Drug prescription services
- Catheter management (Foley's catheter removal and insertion)
- Ryle's tube removal and insertion
- Blood sample collection
- Nutritional counselling
- Patient care education for caregivers (including cleaning, bathing, oral care, wound dressing, and catheter care)
- Provision of necessary documentation for deployment of special furnishings (hospital beds, wheelchairs, airbeds)

**Scale**: At the time of the 2022 study by Taj and Suzana, Dhamanaveshi catered for 37 registered clients and Hulhumale Hospital for 30 registered clients in the Greater Male' region -- a total of just 67 bedridden patients (at the time of the 2022 study).[^41] Between 2019 and 2021, the program facilitated 93 home visits/monitoring sessions for elderly bedridden patients nationwide.[^42] These numbers suggest that the program, while valued, operates at a very small scale relative to need.

### 7.46.2 Research Evidence on the Home Visit Program

The first formal evaluation of the home visit program was conducted by Amir Taj and Mariyam Suzana (2023), published in *The Maldives National Journal of Research*. Their quantitative descriptive study, carried out from February to July 2022, surveyed 123 caretakers of all registered bedridden patients in the Greater Male' region using a 49-item research instrument.[^43]

**Patient Profile**: The bedridden patients had a mean age of 81.4 years (SD: 8.02), with a modal age of 82 years. The overwhelming majority (79%) were female. More than half (57%) were diagnosed with cerebrovascular diseases, followed by cardiovascular disease (11%), endocrine conditions (11%), respiratory conditions (7%), and others including dyslipidemia, chronic kidney disease, and cancer (13%). Eighty-five percent were fully bedridden, 84% were on more than three medicines, 78% were on nappies, 28% were on Ryle's tube, and 19% had bedsores.[^44]

**Caretaker Profile**: The most striking finding about caretakers is the dominance of family members, particularly children. Sixty-seven percent of bedridden patients were cared for by their sons or daughters, and only 10% were cared for by hired caretakers. The majority of caretakers (69%) were female, 75% were married, and 59% were employed (juggling caregiving with work). Critically, **78% of caretakers had received no training** in caring for bedridden patients. The preferred place of care was overwhelmingly the patient's own home (93%).[^45]

**Satisfaction Findings**: Overall satisfaction with the program was high -- the median score was 10 out of 10 (IQR: 2) for the overall program, and 11 out of 14 (IQR: 7) for services. Caretakers were particularly satisfied with drug prescription services, general consultation, and general examination services (satisfaction rates ranging from 41% to 74% across service indicators).[^46]

However, satisfaction was dramatically lower for two critical dimensions:

- **Procedures** conducted during home visits: median satisfaction score of just **2 out of 12** (IQR: 6). Catheter changing frequency and quality and dressing procedures drew the most dissatisfaction.
- **Training** provided to caretakers: median satisfaction score of only **5 out of 10** (IQR: 8). Training on providing proper diet, mental care, building self-confidence, and improving mobility all received low satisfaction scores (ranging from 24% to 38%).[^47]

The frequency of home visits was statistically significantly associated with satisfaction (p = 0.024), with those receiving more frequent visits being more satisfied. The study also found that the majority of patients (67%) did not live with their families, suggesting a pattern where family members provide care but may not reside in the same household -- a reflection of the housing pressures in the Greater Male' region.[^48]

### 7.46.3 Community-Based Support and Its Limitations

Beyond the formal home visit program, community-based elderly support in the Maldives relies heavily on informal networks. The Family and Children Service Centres (FCSCs), present at the atoll level, play a role in addressing the needs of vulnerable elderly persons in rural and isolated communities. However, the MIPAA survey acknowledged that "due to the limited budget, FCSC's are not able to fully cater to the needs of older people at risk in all islands."[^49]

Three civil society organisations were identified in the 2021 MIPAA survey as working on elderly issues:[^50]

1. **Aged Care Maldives**: A nonprofit focusing on the needs of the senior population and their families, promoting healthy ageing and encouraging elders to be contributing community members.
2. **Dhivehi Israhvehinge Jamiyya** (Maldives Senior Citizens Association): A nonprofit working to create awareness on ageing and elderly rights.
3. **G-Care Maldives**: A nonprofit working for elderly rights and well-being, with active members teaching skills and activities for home-based elderly, and providing transportation services for elderly people before the pandemic.

However, the government's 2021 assessment noted that it "does not provide any sustainable financial support for these organizations, however technical support and platforms are created for these organizations to showcase their activities and increase public awareness."[^51] The reliance on unfunded civil society organisations for community-based elderly support represents a significant structural weakness.

---


## 7.47 The Taj and Suzana (2023) Home Visit Study: Comprehensive Findings


### 7.47.1 Study Context and Significance

The Taj and Suzana (2023) study, published in *The Maldives National Journal of Research* (Vol. 11, No. 1, July 2023, pp. 53-66), represents the **first and only evaluation** of the government's Home Visit Programme since its inception in 2015. The authors – Amir Taj from Dhamanaveshi (Ministry of Health) and Mariyam Suzana from the Maldives National University – conducted a quantitative descriptive study from **February to July 2022**, interviewing 102 caretakers (sampled from 123 available) at the two implementing centres: Dhamanaveshi and the Public Health Unit of Hulhumale Hospital. The study achieved a response rate of 92%. Ethics approval was obtained from the Maldives National Health Research Council (Ref No: NHRC/2022/07) and the Maldives National University Research Ethics Committee (Ref No: RE/2022/B-03). Notably, the principal investigator (Taj) is himself part of Dhamanaveshi's home visit team – providing insider knowledge of programme operations but also necessitating the conflict-of-interest disclosure that he was "not involved in any data collection nor directly contacted the relatives of the bedridden patients during the research time period."[^342]

### 7.47.2 Registration and Delivery Architecture

The programme operates through a dual-centre model. **Families must register patients through the Ministry of Gender and Families**, after which the Home Visit Programme is conducted in collaboration with the Ministry of Health. At the time of the study, Dhamanaveshi catered for **37 registered clients** and Hulhumale Hospital for **30 registered clients** – totalling the 67 bedridden patients (at the time of the 2022 study) documented elsewhere in this chapter. The registration process is atoll-level: the MIPAA survey documented that registration occurs through Health Centres and Island Council Offices, with Dhamanaveshi and the Public Health Unit of Hulhumale Hospital serving as the implementing centres in Greater Male'.[^343]

### 7.47.3 The Visit Frequency Gap: Design vs. Delivery

The study reveals a critical discrepancy between the programme's designed frequency and its actual delivery. The literature review section of the study states that **"routine visits occur twice a week"** – the programme's intended visit frequency. However, the actual data from the 123 patients shows:

| Visit Frequency | Number | Percentage |
|---|---|---|
| Once or less per month | 50 | 40.7% |
| Twice per month | 68 | 55.3% |
| Three or more times per month | 5 | 4.1% |

The gap between the designed frequency (approximately 8 visits per month) and the actual delivery (1-2 visits per month for 96% of patients) represents a **fourfold to eightfold shortfall** in service delivery. For patients requiring catheter changes, wound dressing, tube management, and nutritional monitoring, the difference between twice-weekly and once-monthly visits is clinically significant. The visit frequency was found to be statistically significant for caretaker satisfaction (p = 0.024), confirming that less frequent visits directly reduced satisfaction.[^344]

### 7.47.4 Patient Profile: Age, Gender, and Medical Complexity

The study provides the most detailed clinical profile of the Maldives' bedridden elderly population:

**Demographics:** Patient age ranged from **65 to 99 years**, with a mean age of **81.4 years** (SD 8.02) and a modal age of **82 years**. Females constituted **79%** of patients. Male patients had slightly higher satisfaction rates (92.3% vs 80.4% for females).

**Living arrangements:** **67% of bedridden patients do not live with their families** – a finding that contradicts the cultural assumption that Maldivian elderly are cared for within family structures. For bedridden elderly specifically, the majority are living outside family settings, presumably alone or with hired caretakers.

**Disease profile:**
| Diagnosed Condition | Patients | Percentage |
|---|---|---|
| Cerebrovascular (strokes) | 70 | 56.9% |
| Cardiovascular | 14 | 11.4% |
| Endocrine | 14 | 11.4% |
| Respiratory | 9 | 7.3% |
| Others (dyslipidemia, CKD, cancer) | 16 | 13.0% |

Strokes are overwhelmingly the dominant cause of bedridden status among the elderly, accounting for more than half of all cases. This has direct implications for prevention: investment in hypertension screening and management, blood pressure medication adherence, and stroke risk factor reduction could significantly reduce the future demand for bedridden home care.[^345]

### 7.47.5 Medical Equipment and Physical Condition

The clinical complexity of the bedridden population is substantial:

- **85% fully bedridden** (only 4.9% able to move alone, 10.6% mobile with support)
- **84% on more than three medicines** simultaneously
- **78% on nappies** (incontinence)
- **28% on Ryle's tube** (nasogastric feeding tube)
- **19.5% on Foley's catheter** (urinary catheter)
- **19% with bedsores** (pressure ulcers)

These figures demonstrate that the bedridden elderly population requires skilled nursing care – catheter management, tube feeding, wound care, medication administration – not merely social visits. The high prevalence of bedsores (19%) indicates inadequate repositioning and skin care, a preventable condition that causes significant pain and infection risk.[^346]

### 7.47.6 Complete Service List
