This framework was specifically developed for the SIDS context – a novel conceptualisation that recognises the distinctive socio-economic and geo-spatial conditions of small island developing states. The study found that **health and social connectedness were the most important determinants of wellbeing**, each having a large impact, while economic status, ability to conform with social values, and access to goods and services had small but significant correlations. The primacy of social connectedness (29% shared contribution with wellbeing) over economic status has profound policy implications: it suggests that improving elderly wellbeing in the Maldives depends more on strengthening social networks and enabling social engagement than on increasing income transfers – though both are necessary.[^420]

### 7.98.2 The REHApp Clarification

An earlier reference to the Maldives piloting the REHApp (Rehabilitation App) mobile application for family caregivers of elderly persons requires correction. Research confirms that **REHApp is not specifically connected to Maldives elderly care.** REHApp, developed by Enablement B.V. (a Dutch non-profit) in 2017, with Version 2.0 released in 2022 in partnership with Plan International, is a **disability rehabilitation knowledge base** for fieldworkers in low- and middle-income countries. It is organised around the International Classification of Functioning, Disability and Health (ICF) framework, with chapters covering amputation, burns, clubfoot, leprosy, low vision, and spinal cord injury. It is not designed for elderly caregivers specifically. The Maldives appears only in the broader MEDBOX platform's geographic index but receives no specific mention in relation to REHApp deployment. While the app's offline capability and free availability would make it theoretically useful in a SIDS context, no evidence was found of its adoption or piloting for elderly care in the Maldives.[^421]

---


---


## 7.99 Ageing in the Atolls: Jaufar and Ibrahim (2025)


### 7.99.1 Study Design and Context

Contrasting the urban focus of Nazra and Azeez, a 2025 phenomenological study by A. Jaufar and F. Ibrahim – "Lived Experiences of Ageing and Well-Being among Older Adults Living in the Rural Maldives," published in the *International Journal of Social Research & Innovation* (Volume 8, Issue 1, 2025) – examined the experience of ageing in rural Maldivian island communities through in-depth interviews with fifteen seniors on outer atolls.[^107]

### 7.99.2 Key Findings

**Preference for Ageing in Place.** Participants expressed a strong and consistent preference for ageing on their home islands, citing deep emotional connections to place and the social life centred around the mosque. Unlike the Male' respondents in Nazra's study – who were confined to apartments – rural elderly remained embedded in island community life, visiting the mosque daily and maintaining informal social networks.[^108]

**Intergenerational Reciprocity.** A distinctive finding was the pattern of intergenerational exchange: elderly participants cared for grandchildren in exchange for household help from younger family members. This reciprocal arrangement maintained the dignity and purpose of older persons while providing practical childcare support – a model absent in the Male' urban context where elderly were more likely to be passive recipients of care.[^109]

**Financial Concerns.** Financial worries centred not on the adequacy of the basic pension per se but on the rising costs of food and fuel that were outpacing pension income. On outer islands, imported goods are significantly more expensive than in Male' due to transport costs, and fuel prices affect both household energy and the fishing livelihoods that supplement pension income. The gap between pension purchasing power and island cost of living was widening.[^110]

**Inter-Island Transport as a Health Barrier.** Access to medical care was constrained by a lack of accessible inter-island ferries. For elderly with mobility limitations, the physical demands of boarding and disembarking from dhonis (traditional boats) and speedboats were prohibitive. This transport barrier meant that referrals to atoll hospitals or tertiary facilities in Male' were delayed, deferred, or foregone entirely – particularly for chronic disease management that required regular follow-up.[^111]

**Loneliness as Exception, Not Norm.** Unlike the Male' study where 55.4% reported loneliness, feelings of loneliness in the rural sample were uncommon. Only two of fifteen respondents felt socially isolated, and both attributed this to their children's migration to Male' for work. The mosque, the island cafe, and neighbourhood interactions provided sufficient daily social contact for most participants. This finding sharply delineates the urban-rural divide in elderly wellbeing.[^112]

**Fear of Becoming a Burden.** Nearly all participants worried about becoming a "burden" if they developed a serious disability, as formal homecare services were unavailable in the atolls. The absence of any community health worker or home-based care programme on their islands meant that a health crisis would require either relocation to Male' (severing all community ties) or placing impossible caregiving demands on family members who were themselves ageing or had migrated.[^113]

### 7.99.3 Policy Implications

The authors argued that ageing policy must be tailored to the specific needs of island communities, not simply extrapolated from Male'-centric models. Their recommendations included mobile health teams that visit outer islands on a regular circuit, age-friendly inter-island transportation (accessible ferries with seating, railings, and covered areas), and community-based social activities organised through island councils. The study demonstrated that the strengths of island life – community cohesion, mosque-centred socialisation, intergenerational reciprocity – could be leveraged as foundations for ageing-in-place policy, rather than attempting to replicate institutional models designed for urban continental settings.[^114]

---


## 7.100 Rural Elderly Experience: Deeper Findings from Jaufar and Ibrahim (2025)


### 7.100.1 Financial Concerns: Pension Purchasing Power Erosion

Jaufar and Ibrahim's (2025) interviews with fifteen rural elderly residents revealed a dimension of pension inadequacy not captured by the aggregate poverty-line comparisons. Participants reported that **rising costs of food and fuel were outpacing the basic pension** on outer islands. While the MVR 5,000 monthly pension may exceed the national poverty line by 222%, the effective purchasing power on remote islands – where goods must be transported by sea, fuel costs are higher, and the range of available products is limited – erodes this advantage. The pension's fixed nominal value has not been inflation-adjusted since the increase to MVR 5,000, meaning its real value has declined continuously. For outer-island elderly who depend on the pension as their primary or sole income source, this purchasing power erosion translates into tangible hardship: inability to afford adequate nutrition, medication not covered by Aasandha, or the transport costs of visiting family members on other islands.[^287]

### 7.100.2 The Migration-Loneliness Link: Specificity of Isolation

Of the fifteen rural respondents, **only two felt socially isolated – and both attributed their isolation specifically to their children's migration to Male' for work.** This finding is important for two reasons. First, it confirms that social isolation among rural elderly is not generalised but is triggered by the specific event of children's out-migration – the rural social fabric (mosque, community gatherings, neighbour relationships) generally sustains social connection for the elderly as long as they have at least some family nearby. Second, it suggests that interventions could be narrowly targeted: supporting communication between migrated children and their parents (through digital literacy, subsidised phone/video calls) and creating local substitutes for the family support function (through community volunteer programmes) could address the isolation experienced by the specific subset of rural elderly whose children have left.[^288]

### 7.100.3 The Fear of Disability-Related Dependency

Nearly all fifteen participants expressed worry about becoming a **"burden" if they developed a serious disability**, as formal homecare services are unavailable in the atolls. This near-universal fear – shared even by participants who were currently healthy and socially connected – reveals the psychological weight of knowing that one health event (a stroke, a fall, progressive vision loss) could transform an independent, community-integrated elder into someone entirely dependent on family members who may themselves have limited capacity to provide care. On outer islands where there is no nurse, no physiotherapist, no home care aide, and no accessible transport to regional hospitals, the trajectory from health event to dependency to potential abandonment or transfer to Guraidhoo is understood by the elderly as a direct and realistic possibility.[^289]

### 7.100.4 Author Recommendations: Mobile Health Teams and Age-Friendly Transport

Jaufar and Ibrahim argued that ageing policy must be tailored to the specific needs of island communities and recommended two specific interventions: **mobile health teams** that could travel to outer islands on a regular circuit, providing geriatric health assessments, chronic disease management, and basic rehabilitation services; and **age-friendly transportation**, including accessible inter-island ferries with ramps, seating, and weather protection designed for elderly passengers with mobility limitations. These recommendations addressed the two most significant barriers to rural elderly health access: the absence of health professionals on small islands and the physical impossibility of boarding conventional dhonis and speedboats for many elderly persons with mobility impairment.[^290]

---


## 7.101 The 2013-2018 Government Report on Elderly Rights


### 7.101.1 Historical Context

The Ministry of Gender and Family's 2018 report, "Promotion and Protection of the Rights of Children, Women, Elderly and Persons with Disabilities 2013-2018," provides the historical record of the formative period in Maldivian elderly policy. The report documented that the 2006 National Census Analysis projected the ageing process to begin accelerating from 2025 and to reach critical mass by 2050, when the large baby cohorts of the 1980s reach age 65. Projections based on the Census 2014 indicated that the proportion of elderly persons (5% in 2014) would "almost triple by 2054, reaching 14% of resident population."[^205]

### 7.101.2 The 2018 National Elderly Forum

The report documented the convening of a two-day National Elderly Forum on 15-16 August 2018, with two objectives: (1) high-level policy dialogue on healthy ageing between the Ministry of Gender and Family and the Ministry of Health, and (2) production of a two-year result-based National Elderly Action Plan. Day 1 featured ministerial-level policy dialogue; Day 2 comprised policy and technical level discussions to validate the NEAP. The Forum consulted with the Ministry of Health, Society for Health Education (SHE), NSPA, MPAO, Maldives Red Crescent (MRC), Dhivehiraajeyge Israhvehing Jamiyya (DIJ), and Dhamanaveshi – demonstrating a multi-stakeholder approach to elderly policy development that was, at the time, novel in the Maldivian context.[^206]

### 7.101.3 International Engagement

The report documented the Maldives' international engagement on elderly rights during the period, including participation in the Help-Age Asia Pacific Conference 2018 (23-25 October, Tehran), themed "Family, Community and State in Ageing Societies"; the Asia-Pacific Intergovernmental Meeting on the Third Review of MIPAA (12-14 September 2017, Bangkok); and the Asia-Pacific Ministerial Forum on Management of Social Transformations (22-23 March 2017, Malaysia). These engagements connected Maldivian policymakers with regional and global elderly care expertise and frameworks.[^207]

---


## 7.102 Deeper Analysis of the 2018 Government Report on Elderly Programming


### 7.102.1 The National Elderly Policy Launch: Specific Details

The 2018 government report "Promotion and Protection of the Rights of Children, Women, Elderly and Persons with Disabilities 2013-2018" provides essential operational detail about the early period of Maldivian elderly policy that is not available from other sources. The National Elderly Policy was formally launched on **1 October 2017** – the International Day of Older Persons – by **Vice President H.E. Abdulla Jihad**. The timing was deliberate: linking the domestic policy launch to the international observance signalled the government's commitment to aligning Maldivian elderly policy with global frameworks.[^213]

The report clarified the Ministry's seven-point mandate for elderly care, which comprised: (1) formulate, implement, and monitor policies related to elderly welfare; (2) assure social protection for elderly persons; (3) coordinate with state authorities on elderly service delivery; (4) identify emerging issues affecting the elderly population; (5) conduct public awareness campaigns on elderly rights and ageing; (6) provide psychosocial support to elderly persons and their families; and (7) raise awareness at island and atoll levels about elderly care obligations and services. This mandate established the Ministry as the lead coordinating body for all elderly-related policy – a role that would be formally codified in the 2024 National Ageing Policy revision.[^214]

### 7.102.2 The Ranveyla Campaign: Expanded Detail

The Ranveyla campaign, launched in **August 2016**, was broader in scope than a simple health screening programme. The 2018 report documented that the campaign comprised two distinct components conducted at the atoll level: (a) health screening and awareness sessions specifically for senior citizens, and (b) interactive sessions designed to "bridge the inter-generational gap between youth and senior citizens." The campaign represented the first systematic attempt by the Ministry to address both the health dimension and the social inclusion dimension of elderly care through a single programme deployed across multiple atolls. The atoll-by-atoll data on the number of health screenings and intergenerational sessions conducted was documented in a visual map format in the report, demonstrating geographic reach across the archipelago.[^215]

### 7.102.3 Home Visit Programme Registration Numbers

The 2018 report stated that as of the reporting period, **93 persons were registered (as of 2018)** in the Home Visit Programme in the Greater Male' region. This figure is significant because it differs from the 67 patients reported in the 2021 MIPAA survey and the Taj and Suzana (2023) study, which cited 37 patients at Dhamanaveshi and 30 at Hulhumale Hospital. The discrepancy likely reflects different reporting periods and potential attrition – some registered patients may have died, moved, or recovered between the reporting dates. The programme was established at "the end of 2015" at Dhamanaveshi, earlier than some other sources have suggested.[^216]

### 7.102.4 Professional Development Training on Elderly Service Provision

A critical but overlooked capacity-building initiative was the Ministry's "Professional Development Training on Service Provisions for the Elderly and Victims of Abuse," conducted with WHO support across three atolls. The training programme was delivered in three rounds: (1) 24 health and social workers from Vaavu Atoll, Meemu Atoll, and Faafu Atoll participated in a 5-day programme held in Faafu Atoll Nilandhoo from 11 to 15 March; (2) 33 persons from Alif Alif and Alif Dhaalu Atolls completed the programme from 8 to 12 April 2018; and (3) 14 health and social workers participated in Addu City. A total of **71 health and social workers were trained** under the programme, which covered relevant laws and regulations, procedures for providing protection to children, women, elderly, and persons with disabilities, and standards for the provision of support and care. This represented the first systematic training programme for frontline workers specifically incorporating elderly care competencies, though the training combined elderly care with broader protection mandates rather than focusing exclusively on geriatric issues.[^217]

### 7.102.5 Case Volume and Elderly Abuse in the Ministry Caseload

The Ministry reported receiving an average of **200 cases per month** on various issues of neglect, abuse, and violence against vulnerable groups – explicitly including children, women, elderly, and persons with disabilities. While the report did not disaggregate the 200 monthly cases by population group, the inclusion of elderly as one of four named vulnerable groups confirmed that elder abuse formed a routine part of the Ministry's caseload, consistent with the 270 elderly abuse cases reported to MoGFSS between 2018 and 2021 in the MIPAA survey.[^218]

### 7.102.6 Community Social Groups and Elderly Welfare

The report documented that Community Social Groups (CSGs), known locally as IBAMA, were established with an explicit mandate that included elderly assistance. The CSGs operate at the island level and serve as community-based mechanisms for identifying and responding to the needs of vulnerable populations. The inclusion of elderly welfare in the CSG mandate provided a decentralised service delivery mechanism for elderly support on outer islands – a complement to the centralised Ministry services in Male'. The 2024 National Ageing Policy would later reference IBAMA as one of the foundational institutions upon which community-level elderly support could be built.[^219]

---


## 7.103 The 2018 Government Report: Remaining Operational Detail


### 7.103.1 Ranveyla Campaign: Atoll-Level Deployment

The 2018 government report included a visual map documenting the geographic deployment of the Ranveyla campaign across atolls. Each atoll was marked with specific counts of: (a) health screening and awareness sessions for senior citizens, and (b) sessions for youth to bridge the intergenerational gap with elderly. While the specific numbers per atoll were presented in a map format that is difficult to reproduce in text, the key finding is that the campaign achieved multi-atoll coverage – it was not confined to Male' or a few urban centres but was deployed across the archipelago's decentralised geography. This geographic reach distinguishes the Ranveyla campaign from most other elderly programmes, which have been concentrated in the Greater Male' region.[^314]

### 7.103.2 The NEAP: WHO Support and Finalisation Status

The 2018 report stated that the National Elderly Action Plan was compiled "as part of the National Elderly Forum 2018" and that the plan was **"supported by WHO in 2018"** but was **"yet to be finalized"** at the time of the report's publication. This clarifies the timeline: the two-day National Elderly Forum (15-16 August 2018) produced a draft NEAP; WHO provided technical support for the drafting process; but the plan was not formally endorsed until 2019 – when it became the "National Plan of Action on Older Persons 2019-2023." The fact that the plan required over a year from initial drafting (August 2018) to formal endorsement (November 2019) illustrates the slow pace of elderly policy development even when dedicated institutional attention and international technical support are available.[^315]

### 7.103.3 The HelpAge Conference Breakout Sessions

The 2018 report documented Maldivian participation in the Help-Age Asia Pacific Conference 2018 in Tehran, noting the conference's three breakout session streams: **social inclusion, income security, and long-term care**. These three streams correspond directly to the three major challenges identified throughout this chapter: social isolation among Male's elderly (addressed by the social inclusion stream), pension sustainability and adequacy (income security), and the near-total absence of formal care services (long-term care). The conference exposed Maldivian policymakers to regional approaches in all three areas, potentially informing the subsequent development of the 2024 revised National Ageing Policy.[^316]

---


## 7.104 The 2018 Report: Government's Own Characterisation of Elderly Marginalisation


### 7.104.1 The Ministry's Framing of the Problem

The opening paragraph of the 2018 government report's Chapter 1 (Elderly Rights) contains the government's own characterisation of the elderly situation, which is significant because it represents the state's institutional acknowledgment – not merely an academic finding – of elderly marginalisation:

**"Older persons in the country, often find themselves marginalized from families and societies, especially after retirement. Older persons also, often depend on their families to act as informal caregivers and provide necessary assistance for their health and well-being. As their economic and social roles shift, these can have adverse psychological impacts on their health, particularly for those who become more dependent on their families. This increases their vulnerability, compounds the problems of isolation further and affects their independence and participation."**

This government-authored passage acknowledges four interconnected dynamics: (a) marginalisation from families and society post-retirement; (b) dependency on families as informal caregivers; (c) adverse psychological impacts of role shifts and dependency; and (d) compounding isolation that undermines independence and participation. The government's own analysis thus confirms what the academic studies by Nazra (2018) and Azeez et al. (2020) subsequently documented empirically.[^352]

### 7.104.2 The 2006 Census Projection Discrepancy

The 2018 report cited the 2006 National Census Analysis as projecting that the elderly proportion would reach **"15 to 20 percent in 2055."** This is notably higher than the 2014 Population Projections, which estimated 14% by 2054. The discrepancy may reflect different methodological assumptions between the two projection exercises, but the 2006 estimate's higher range (up to 20%) has proved more consistent with subsequent UN projections, which estimate 21.5% aged 65+ by 2050. The 2006 analysis also projected that the ageing process would "embark" in 2025 and "accelerate in 2050 when the large baby cohorts of the 1980s reach the age of 65" – a specific demographic mechanism linking the high-fertility era of the 1980s to the future ageing peak.[^353]

### 7.104.3 The Ranveyla Campaign Report 2016-2018

The 2018 government report references a separate Ministry publication – the **"Ranveyla Campaign Report 2016-2018"** – which documents the campaign's achievements in detail. This publication, not otherwise referenced in this chapter, represents an additional primary source on the campaign's reach, activities, and outcomes. The Ranveyla Campaign itself aimed to "increase participation of elderly persons within family and community, enhance care and support provided for them, and improve their health and emotional well-being" through two mechanisms: (a) health screenings and awareness sessions for senior citizens at the community level, and (b) interactive sessions to bridge the intergenerational gap between youth and senior citizens. The campaign's reach was documented in an atoll-by-atoll map showing deployment across the entire archipelago.[^354]

---

---


---


## 7.105 The Ageing Ungracefully Article: Remaining Detail


### 7.105.1 The G-Care Interview: Elderly Who Want to Socialise

The Maldives Financial Review's "Ageing Ungracefully" article included an interview with G-Care organisers that provided ground-level detail about elderly social aspirations. According to the G-Care organisers, **"there are many elderly in Male' who want to be actively involved and socialise but are unable to do so."** The primary barrier was not lack of desire but physical infrastructure: **"the lack of lifts, or other accessibility facilities, in buildings which keep them isolated in their homes, finding it too difficult, or impossible, to navigate the many flights of stairs up and down."** Additionally, the article described the urban mobility challenge: **"they have to depend on taxis to get to activities and social outings but given the traffic congestion on the streets of Male' and the incessant beeping and horning whenever a taxi stops to take on a passenger, the elderly are reluctant to venture outside."** Walking was equally problematic: **"Walking to their destination is also difficult, given the congestion and the lack of pedestrian space, bringing with it difficulties in navigating the streets, especially for the elderly."**[^317]

### 7.105.2 The Television and Childcare Default

The article described the daily reality of many elderly persons in Male' with particular vividness: many elderly **"depend on their families for social interaction, stuck as they are in their homes. However, with almost all members of the family working to sustain the generally high cost of living in Male', and each busy with their own lives, they are left to fend for themselves, usually alone in high-rise buildings, with the television switched on, or even left to care for children."** This passage captures two distinct forms of invisibility: the elderly person alone with the television (social isolation through neglect) and the elderly person left to care for children (social isolation through exploitation of their availability as unpaid labour). Both forms are enabled by the same structural condition: the economic necessity that drives all working-age family members into employment, leaving the elderly as either passive occupants of empty apartments or default childcare providers.[^318]

### 7.105.3 The COVID-19 and Ageism Observation

The article made a pointed observation about COVID-19 and ageism: **"The COVID-19 pandemic has brought with it its own form of ageism – perhaps reflecting callously that someone over some imaginary line has 'lived a full life.' The nation continues to be immune to the possibilities life will have to offer for someone who is 65 or older – tantamount to systemic myopia given that the life expectancy in the Maldives is currently 87, meaning one is left with no recourse but to spend 27 years, stuck inside increasingly shrinking physical, and mental, space and isolated from the world."** The life expectancy figure of 87 years cited here appears to be an error or projection. [Note: The 87-year figure appears to be an error in the original source; UN DESA World Population Prospects 2022 reports life expectancy at birth of 81.1 years.], but the rhetorical point remains valid: regardless of the exact figure, a person retiring at 65 faces potentially 15-25 years of life for which no institutional support exists beyond a pension and health insurance. The article concluded: **"The nation owes these senior representatives better, if not for the services that they have provided to society and the nation, for the fact that eventually every young citizen will also need to face this march of time. Everyone deserves to face this march with grace and human dignity."**[^319]

### 7.105.4 The WDC Recommendation: Historical Precedent

The article specifically recommended that **"city, atoll and island councils, as well as the women's development committee could become involved in integrating the elderly, similar to the ward committees in Male' and other islands, during the 1970s and 1980s."** This recommendation is notable for invoking historical precedent – the ward committees of the Nasir and early Gayoom eras served as community-level social welfare mechanisms, and the article suggests that contemporary institutions (councils, WDCs) could revive this function for elderly welfare. The IBAMA community-based system, documented in §7.49, represents a partial realisation of this recommendation, though its implementation for elderly welfare specifically remains undocumented.[^320]

---


## 7.106 Research Evidence and Policy Recommendations


### 7.106.1 Summary of the Evidence Base

The evidence base on elderly care in the Maldives is thin but growing. The key sources include:

1. **The 2021 MIPAA Voluntary National Survey** -- the most comprehensive official self-assessment, revealing both policy achievements and candid implementation gaps
2. **Taj and Suzana (2023)** -- the only peer-reviewed study of the home visit program, providing detailed quantitative data on patient and caretaker characteristics and satisfaction
3. **Nazra (2018)** -- a qualitative study of 8 older adults in Male', providing rare direct testimony about lived experience
4. **The Ministry of Social and Family Development's 2024 presentation** -- the most current demographic and epidemiological data on population ageing
5. **HE Aishath Mohamed Didi's 2022 MIPAA statement** -- articulating the government's policy commitments and gender-lens approach
6. **The vulnerability mapping exercise** -- providing data on pension amounts, eligibility, and coverage within the broader social protection framework

The "Ageing Ungracefully" article in the Maldives Financial Review (June 2021) highlighted the public discourse around ageism, inadequate aged care, and the projected fivefold increase in the elderly population by 2054.[^83] These public discussions reflect a growing awareness that the current pace of institutional preparation is insufficient for the demographic changes ahead.

### 7.106.2 Critical Gaps Identified

Based on the synthesis of available evidence, the following critical gaps emerge:

**Legislative Gap**: The absence of a dedicated elderly law leaves older persons without specific legal protections. The existing legal framework addresses elderly welfare only tangentially through instruments designed for other purposes (domestic violence, general social protection, general health insurance). An Elderly Rights Act is urgently needed to establish age-specific legal protections, define elder abuse and neglect, mandate reporting obligations, set care standards, and create enforcement mechanisms.

**Institutional Care Deficit**: With only one residential facility (HPSN on Guraidhoo), the Maldives has negligible institutional care capacity. The planned facilities in Male' and Addu, even if completed, would not come close to meeting projected demand. A comprehensive long-term care strategy is needed that encompasses residential, day care, respite care, and community-based options.

**Geriatric Health Services Gap**: The complete absence of geriatric medicine as a specialty, combined with the lack of trained geriatric nurses and caregivers, means that the healthcare system is structurally unprepared for the health needs of an ageing population. The planned Level 2/3 courses on elderly care are a start but fall far short of building the specialised workforce needed.

**Caregiver Support Gap**: The 78% of family caregivers who have received no training represent a massive unmet need. Caregivers need not only training but also respite care services, financial support, and recognition of the economic value of unpaid care work. The day care component of the planned Addu facility is designed to address this, but much more is needed.

**Data and Research Gap**: The limited data on the elderly population -- their health status, economic circumstances, living arrangements, unmet needs, and subjective well-being -- makes evidence-based policymaking extremely difficult. The nationwide situational analysis commissioned from Villa College is a step in the right direction but should be followed by regular, systematic data collection.

**Geographic Equity Gap**: The concentration of services in Male' and the planned expansion to Addu still leaves the majority of the archipelago without accessible elderly care infrastructure. Atoll-level responses -- potentially building on the FCSC network -- are needed to ensure that elderly persons on outer islands are not left without support.

**Housing Gap**: The exclusion of elderly persons from social housing schemes is a significant policy gap, particularly given that 60% of the Male' population lives in rented accommodation and elderly persons on fixed pensions are particularly vulnerable to housing insecurity.

**Disaster Preparedness Gap**: The absence of any age-specific disaster response planning, acknowledged in the MIPAA survey, is particularly concerning given the Maldives' extreme climate vulnerability.

### 7.106.3 Policy Recommendations

Drawing on the evidence reviewed in this chapter, the following policy recommendations are offered:

**Short-Term (1-3 years)**:

1. **Enact the Elderly Rights and Protection Act**: Complete and pass the dedicated law on elderly rights that has been in draft since at least 2021, establishing specific legal protections, mandatory reporting of elder abuse, and care standards.

2. **Scale Up the Home Visit Program**: Expand the bedridden home visit program beyond its current minimal scale (67 patients in Greater Male' as of 2022) to all inhabited islands, with increased visit frequency and mandatory caregiver training components.

3. **Establish a National Registry of Elderly Persons**: Create a comprehensive database of all persons aged 65+, including their health status, living arrangements, care needs, and support networks, to enable targeted service delivery and planning.

4. **Launch Caregiver Training and Support Programs**: Develop and deliver standardised training for family caregivers, including practical caregiving skills, mental health support, and information about available services and entitlements.

5. **Include Elderly in Social Housing Eligibility**: Remove the exclusion of elderly persons from social housing schemes and develop age-friendly housing options in Hulhumale' and other planned urban developments.

**Medium-Term (3-7 years)**:

6. **Complete the Addu and Male' Elderly Care Facilities**: Bring the planned residential and day care facilities to completion, with staffing and operational models informed by international best practice and adapted to the Maldivian context.

7. **Develop Geriatric Medicine Capacity**: Send Maldivian physicians abroad for geriatric medicine training, establish geriatric units at IGMH and regional hospitals, and integrate geriatric assessment into primary healthcare protocols.

8. **Build Atoll-Level Elderly Support Services**: Develop community-based elderly care programs at the atoll level, leveraging the existing FCSC network and training island-level health workers and social workers in elderly care.

9. **Develop Age-Friendly Infrastructure Standards**: Establish building codes and urban design standards that ensure public buildings, transport, and community spaces are accessible and safe for older persons.

10. **Integrate Ageing into Disaster Preparedness**: Develop age-specific disaster response protocols, including evacuation procedures for mobility-impaired elderly, post-disaster mental health support, and continuity of care planning.

**Long-Term (7+ years)**:

11. **Conduct Pension System Actuarial Review**: Commission a comprehensive actuarial review of the basic pension system's long-term fiscal sustainability, including scenario analysis under different demographic projections, and develop a sustainable financing strategy.

12. **Develop a National Dementia Strategy**: As the elderly population grows, the prevalence of dementia will increase significantly. A national strategy encompassing prevention, early diagnosis, treatment, and care is needed.

13. **Establish a National Institute on Ageing**: Create a dedicated research and policy institution focused on population ageing, with a mandate to conduct longitudinal research, inform policy, and build national expertise.

14. **Mainstream Ageing Across Government**: Ensure that all government policies -- from urban planning to climate adaptation to economic development -- are assessed for their impact on the ageing population, reflecting the cross-cutting nature of demographic change.

### 7.106.4 Conclusion

The Maldives finds itself in a race against demographics. The institutional, legal, and service infrastructure for elderly care is being built, but it is being built slowly, with limited resources, and against a demographic clock that is ticking with increasing urgency. The country has established important policy foundations -- a National Elderly Policy, a Plan of Action, a universal basic pension, and a home visit program -- but the gap between these frameworks and their implementation on the ground remains wide.

The fundamental challenge is one of timing and scale. The policy frameworks articulate the right principles, but the speed of demographic change demands a pace of implementation that current resource allocations and institutional capacities cannot support. The window of opportunity -- the period before the large working-age cohort begins to retire and the old-age dependency ratio rises sharply -- is narrowing. Every year of delayed investment in elderly care infrastructure, geriatric health capacity, caregiver support, and data systems is a year closer to a demographic reality for which the country will be inadequately prepared.

The Maldives' experience also illustrates a broader truth about population ageing in small island developing states: the challenges are qualitatively different from those faced by larger continental countries. Geographic dispersion, limited economies of scale, extreme climate vulnerability, and the erosion of traditional family structures by urbanisation and migration create a distinctive set of pressures that require distinctively innovative policy responses. The standard models of residential care, developed for continental urban settings, cannot simply be transplanted to an archipelago of 187 inhabited islands. The Maldives will need to develop its own models -- community-based, technology-enabled, and culturally grounded -- if it is to provide dignified ageing for its rapidly growing elderly population.

---


---


## 7.107 Evidence Gaps: A Systematic Assessment


### 7.107.1 The Action Plans Evidence Cross-Reference

The Action Plans Analysis (Part XIX of this encyclopedia) provides a systematic evidence cross-reference for the National Ageing Policy that catalogues both available evidence and critical data gaps across five domains:

**Development and Mainstreaming:** Available evidence confirms population ageing as the fastest demographic shift (8.3% aged 60+, projected 34.1% by 2054; TFR 1.7). The government's own MIPAA survey acknowledges that "lack of overall political support" for ageing issues is "always" present. **Gap:** No actuarial projection of pension liabilities exists; no age-disaggregated data on service utilisation has been collected.

**Health and Wellbeing:** Available evidence documents 46% hypertension, 28% diabetes, 45% disability among the elderly, and that mental health services are "severely limited" with specialist doctors "scarce in the atolls." **Gap:** No geriatric workforce assessment has been conducted; no NCD management outcome data exists for elderly patients; no palliative care needs assessment has been undertaken.

**Ageing in Place:** Available evidence confirms only one residential facility (HPSN Guraidhoo); planned facilities in Addu and Male' have stalled; no regulatory framework exists for private elderly care; rural-to-urban migration leaves elderly isolated on outer islands. **Gap:** No community-based care capacity mapping; no family caregiver burden assessment; no unmet long-term care needs data.

**Income Security:** Available evidence confirms Basic Pension MVR 5,000/month with near-universal coverage for 65+; 89.9% informal employment (99.1% for women); pension adequacy varies by location (adequate in outer atolls, inadequate in Male'). **Gap:** No actuarial projections exist for the pension system; no pension adequacy analysis by location has been conducted.

**Caregiver Support:** Available evidence confirms primary support persons are "mostly women" with no formal support and no respite services. **Gap:** No caregiver health outcomes data; no time-use data on caregiving burden; no respite care costing studies.[^284]

### 7.107.2 The MIPAA Compliance Assessment

The Action Plans Analysis also maps the National Ageing Policy against MIPAA pillars, identifying three areas of non-compliance:

1. **Older persons in development:** No plan addresses the economic contribution of older persons to Maldivian society.
2. **Advancing health and wellbeing:** Geriatric care infrastructure does not exist outside Greater Male'.
3. **Ensuring enabling and supportive environments:** No age-friendly cities or communities programme has been launched in the Maldives.

The population coverage matrix confirms that the elderly are the **primary focus of only one national plan** (the Ageing Policy), with limited or no coverage in the SAP, GEAP, DVPNS, Health Master Plan, Disability Plan, or Child Protection Policy – confirming the institutional isolation of elderly policy from the broader social policy architecture.[^285]

---


# Part 11: Regional and International Comparisons


## 7.108 Regional and International Comparisons (SAARC)


### 7.108.1 Ageing Across South Asia

Population ageing is not unique to the Maldives within the South Asian region, but the pace and characteristics of the Maldivian transition are distinctive. The UNFPA's 2009 report on population ageing in South Asia highlighted rapid ageing across SAARC nations and called for expanded medical facilities and social services.[^78] However, the speed of the Maldives' fertility decline and the resulting compression of the demographic transition set it apart from larger South Asian countries like India and Bangladesh, where ageing, though accelerating, is proceeding more gradually.

### 7.108.2 The "Growing Old Before Becoming Rich" Challenge

The ADB's analysis of ageing in Sri Lanka coined the phrase "growing old before becoming rich" -- a warning that many Asian countries would face the challenges of aged societies before reaching the income levels that enabled developed countries to build comprehensive elderly care systems.[^79] This framing applies directly to the Maldives, which despite its upper-middle-income classification (largely driven by tourism revenue) faces significant fiscal constraints, particularly in its social sector budgets.

The Maldives' GDP per capita is higher than that of most SAARC nations, which gives it a theoretical advantage in funding elderly care. However, the small population, geographic dispersion, and tourism-dependent economy create cost structures for service delivery that are among the highest in the region on a per-capita basis. Building and staffing a care facility on a remote atoll costs far more per beneficiary than doing so in a continental country with road-connected urban centres.

### 7.108.3 Comparative Pension Systems

The Maldives' universal non-contributory basic pension is relatively generous by SAARC standards. India's National Social Assistance Programme provides means-tested pensions to elderly below the poverty line at much lower amounts (INR 200-500 per month under central government provisions, with state top-ups varying widely). Sri Lanka's elderly allowance is also means-tested and lower in absolute terms. Bangladesh's Old Age Allowance (Boishko Bhata) covers a fraction of the elderly population at modest amounts.

The Maldives' approach -- universal, non-means-tested, and set at a level that represents a meaningful income floor -- is arguably the most progressive basic pension system in South Asia. However, this generosity comes with significant fiscal costs that are proportionally larger than those faced by larger economies, given the Maldives' small tax base and narrow economic structure.

### 7.108.4 Institutional Care Comparisons

Most SAARC countries have similarly underdeveloped institutional care sectors for the elderly, with family-based care remaining the predominant model. India has a growing but largely unregulated private elderly care industry alongside government-run old age homes. Sri Lanka has a network of government and NGO-run elders' homes. Nepal and Bangladesh have minimal institutional care.

The Maldives' unique challenge is the geographic dispersion that makes centralised institutional care impractical for much of the population. While India can establish old age homes in district headquarters accessible to surrounding populations, the Maldives cannot replicate this model across its scattered islands. This geographic reality makes community-based and home-based care models not merely preferable but essential for the Maldivian context.

### 7.108.5 Data Availability and Research

The Maldives' own MIPAA survey acknowledged that "data specific to older persons are not generated but data available for general public" and that "situational analysis and assessment of elderly is underway and this would give a better perspective into the challenges faced by the elderly."[^80] A nationwide assessment on the situation of the elderly population, commissioned from Villa College of Maldives with WHO funding, was reported to be underway with a final report planned by end of February 2022.[^81]

Across the SAARC region, data on elderly populations is generally limited, but the Maldives faces additional challenges due to its small population (making statistically reliable sample surveys more difficult) and geographic dispersion (making data collection expensive). The ADB's 2024 report on elderly poverty in Asia-Pacific highlighted these data gaps as a barrier to evidence-based policy across the region.[^82]

---


## 7.109 Maldives in Regional Comparative Context: The HelpAge/UNFPA Policy Mapping (2015)


### 7.109.1 A Late Starter in the Asia-Pacific

The 2015 HelpAge International/UNFPA Policy Mapping on Ageing in Asia and the Pacific, authored by Camilla Williamson and covering 26 countries, provides the definitive regional comparative context for understanding the Maldives' position in elderly policy development. The mapping, published with UNFPA funding (Publication ID: EAPRDC0022), assessed national plans, legislation, and action plans on ageing across Afghanistan, Bangladesh, Bhutan, Cambodia, China, Cook Islands, DPR Korea, Fiji, India, Indonesia, Iran, Lao PDR, Malaysia, the Maldives, Mongolia, Myanmar, Nepal, Pakistan, Palau, the Philippines, Solomon Islands, Sri Lanka, Thailand, Tonga, Tuvalu, and Viet Nam.[^239]

The Maldives' position in the comparative tables was stark. In Table 1 (national policy, legislation and action plans focused solely on older people), the Maldives row was **entirely blank** – no national plan on ageing, no work and labour policy for the elderly, no migration policy, no education/training, no emergency response, no healthy ageing/NCD policy, no health care access, no healthcare training, no mental health, no nutrition, no living environment, no care and support, no neglect/abuse/rights, and no images of ageing provisions were found as of the September 2014-January 2015 data collection period. This placed the Maldives alongside Afghanistan, Palau, Solomon Islands, Tonga, and Tuvalu as countries with the weakest dedicated ageing policy frameworks in the region.[^240]

### 7.109.2 Mainstream Policy Coverage

In Table 2 (all policy explicitly addressing older people, including mainstream national documents), the Maldives showed somewhat better coverage: Work and Labour (X), Social Protection (X* – indicating a social pension), Disability (X), and Care and Support (X). However, the Maldives still lacked explicit elderly-focused policy on migration, national development plans, education/training, emergency response, healthy ageing/NCDs, health care access, HIV/AIDS, training health care workers, mental health, nutrition, living environment, neglect/abuse/rights, and images of ageing. The X* notation for social protection confirmed the Maldives' social pension (the Basic Pension introduced in 2009/2010), which made the Maldives one of 13 out of 26 countries (50%) in the region with a government-provided, non-contributory cash transfer to older persons.[^241]

### 7.109.3 Maldives-Specific Findings Within the Report

The report's narrative sections contained several Maldives-specific observations. Under section 3.2 on health policy, the report noted: "In the Maldives, the government has introduced policy to decentralise primary health care to achieve similar ends, transferring public health workers, previously employed under the central Ministry of Health and Family, to their respective islands or city councils." This decentralisation of health workers was positioned as relevant to ageing in place on outer islands, though the report noted that the Maldives' NCD and healthy ageing policy contained "no explicit mention of older people."[^242]

On health care access, the report observed that "countries where no policy was found explicitly addressing equal and universal access to health care for older people include Afghanistan, Iran, Maldives, Solomon Islands and Tonga." However, it noted that the Maldives' Aasandha universal health insurance scheme (introduced 2012) covered all citizens, and the National Development Plan 2006-10 aimed for equitable primary health care access – representing population-wide health policy that included but did not specifically target older people.[^243]

### 7.109.4 Maldives as a Late but Rapid Developer

The 2015 mapping captures a Maldives with essentially no dedicated elderly policy infrastructure. The contrast with the 2024-2025 picture – a revised National Ageing Policy with 16 goals, a National Action Plan on Ageing 2025-2028, the Isravvehinge Naadhee senior citizens' centres, the Hattah programme, the N. Kudafari community model, and a developing dementia strategy – represents one of the fastest policy development trajectories on elderly care in the Asia-Pacific region. The Maldives compressed what took most regional comparators a decade or more into approximately seven years (2017-2024), though the implementation gap between policy on paper and services on the ground remains the central challenge.[^244]

---


## 7.110 SIDS Comparative Analysis: How Small Island States Address Ageing


### 7.110.1 The Global SIDS Ageing Context

A 2024 UN DESA Policy Brief (No. 180) provided the definitive global assessment of population ageing in small island developing states. The brief found that in **37 of 57 SIDS**, the 65+ population exceeded 7% of the total population in 2023, with an additional 16 SIDS expected to reach this threshold by 2050. Caribbean SIDS are more advanced in ageing: 90% have 65+ populations at 7% or above, versus approximately 40% in Pacific and Atlantic, Indian Ocean and South China Sea (AIS) groupings. The total 65+ population across all SIDS stood at **6.9 million in 2024**, projected to reach 10 million by the mid-2030s and 15 million by the late 2050s. The brief recommended comprehensive social protection systems, community-based initiatives to bridge gaps in remote islands, strengthened health systems with skilled and retained workforces, expanded digital health and telemedicine for NCD prevention, and a focus on primary healthcare, universal health coverage, and long-term care.[^410]

### 7.110.2 The 2024 Antigua and Barbuda Agenda for SIDS (ABAS)

Adopted unanimously at the SIDS4 Conference in May 2024, the Antigua and Barbuda Agenda for SIDS (ABAS) 2024-2034 identified ageing as a critical challenge for SIDS and called for strong social protection systems for elderly well-being. The ABAS included older persons among vulnerable and marginalised groups whose voices must be heard in policy formulation, alongside persons with disabilities and indigenous peoples. This represents the first SIDS-specific international framework to explicitly name ageing as a priority area.[^411]

### 7.110.3 Country-Level Comparisons

**Seychelles:** The National Policy on Ageing (2016), approved by Cabinet in December 2016, defines elderly as persons aged 63 and above (the national retirement age). With 13,259 persons above 60 (14% of total population), the Seychelles is more advanced in ageing than the Maldives. Notably, 88% of elderly respondents living at home with relatives report feeling safe and cared for. The Seychelles has looked to Sweden's model for elderly care standards.[^412]

**Mauritius:** The National Integrated Care for Older People (ICOPE) Strategic and Action Plan 2022-2026, launched in March 2023, was the first such plan in Africa. Mauritius' population aged 60+ grew from 9% in 2000 to 18.7% in 2021, with projections of 36.5% by 2061. The country provides a **universal non-contributory Basic Retirement Pension (BRP)** for all citizens aged 60+, and in 2025 announced a gradual increase in BRP eligibility from 60 to 65 years. Mauritius leads Africa in proactively identifying ageing as a health and competitiveness issue.[^413]

**Fiji:** A 2022 George Institute report on health system responses to population ageing found the 60+ population expected to triple by 2050. The National Council for Older Persons (NCOPS) coordinates multi-sectoral response but is hindered by limited political leadership, lack of context-appropriate models, and inability to mobilise financial resources. Geographic and gender inequities are pronounced: older men use health services more than older women, and rural/maritime elderly have less access. Informal caregivers need better training and referral pathways.[^414]

**Tonga:** Tonga is the **only small developing island state in the Pacific** with a government-funded social welfare scheme for the elderly – T$65 (US$27) per month for citizens aged 75 and above, costing T$1.4 million (US$582,000) annually. An ADB-assisted Integrated Aged Care Project (Project 55254-001) is helping Tonga develop diversified aged care services. However, long-term care remains under-developed.[^415]

**Samoa:** Samoa provides a universal non-contributory pension of SAT$125/month for all residents over 65, serving as an important additional household income for meeting basic needs and cultural/church obligations.[^415]

**Trinidad and Tobago:** The National Policy on Ageing (2007) addresses 12 priority areas consistent with MIPAA. A dedicated Division of Ageing operates under the Ministry, and a Geriatric Society of Trinidad and Tobago exists. Benefits include free health services, free bus rides, free ferry to Tobago, and free water taxi during non-peak hours for persons 60+. With 13.4% of the population over 60, Trinidad and Tobago is the most institutionally mature SIDS comparator.[^416]

### 7.110.4 The Pacific Health Systems Challenge

Anderson and Irava (2017), writing in *Health Systems & Reform*, assessed the implications of population ageing for Pacific Island health systems. They found that Pacific Island countries have some of the highest rates of obesity and diabetes globally, and that ageing will put pressure across all health system building blocks – leadership/governance, financing, workforce, service delivery, drugs/equipment, and information systems. Critically, Pacific health systems are **"not well equipped" for geriatric conditions** including memory loss, urinary incontinence, depression, and falls/immobility. Sensory organ disease is already the second greatest burden of disease in Fiji, Samoa, and Solomon Islands. The extended family remains the main provider of care and social security but is weakening in urban areas. Anderson and Irava identified a **"strategic window"** for Pacific governments: unlike Asia where ageing is already a substantive challenge, Pacific governments can still plan proactively.[^417]

### 7.110.5 Maldives in the SIDS Comparative Context

The Maldives' position within the SIDS ageing context is distinctive. It shares the Pacific and Caribbean pattern of rapid demographic transition, geographic dispersion, and health system constraints, but differs in several respects: (a) its pension provision (MVR 5,000/month universal basic pension) is more generous in purchasing power terms than most SIDS comparators; (b) its health insurance coverage (Aasandha) is universal, unlike most SIDS; (c) its 2024 National Ageing Policy is relatively recent but comprehensive in scope; and (d) its ageing trajectory is among the fastest in the SIDS grouping, with the 60+ population projected to reach 34.1% by 2054. However, the Maldives lags behind Trinidad and Tobago (which has a dedicated Division of Ageing and Geriatric Society), Mauritius (which has an ICOPE plan), and the Seychelles (which has an adopted National Policy on Ageing since 2016) in institutional development. The Anderson and Irava "strategic window" observation applies directly: the Maldives still has time to build systems before the demographic pressure becomes acute, but this window is closing rapidly.[^418]

## 7.112 Annotated Bibliography of Primary Sources


### Maldives-Specific Research

**Nazra, Aishath (2018).** "Perceptions of Ageing Among Older Adults Living in Male', Maldives and Implications for Provision of Support." *International Journal of Social Research and Innovation* 2(1): 37-60.
*Design:* Qualitative; 8 participants aged 65-70, face-to-face interviews in Male'. Hermeneutic phenomenological analysis. *Key findings:* Six themes – health perception disconnection, autonomy fears, assistive device avoidance, social neglect by society, high-rise confinement, child-like compliance behaviours. No participant aware of any healthy ageing programme. *Significance:* Only qualitative study of elderly perceptions in the Maldives, providing direct verbatim testimony. https://ijsri.villacollege.edu.mv/index.php/ijsri/article/view/24/22

**Azeez, Zahudha Abdul; Imad, Shahla; and Suzana, Mariyam (2020).** "Well-being of the Elderly Living in Male', Maldives: Prevalence and Relationship Among Sociability, Loneliness and Depression." *The Maldives National Journal of Research* 8(2): 31-40.
*Design:* Analytical descriptive; n=312, aged 65-75, four wards of Male'. CES-D, UCLA Loneliness, Eysenck Sociability scales. *Key findings:* 56.1% depression, 55.4% loneliness, 64.0% low sociability. Strong positive loneliness-depression correlation (r=0.817). *Significance:* Largest quantitative study of elderly mental health in the Maldives. https://doi.org/10.62338/xeaetd25

**Jaufar, A. and Ibrahim, F. (2025).** "Lived Experiences of Ageing and Well-Being among Older Adults Living in the Rural Maldives: A Phenomenological Study." *International Journal of Social Research & Innovation* 8(1): 25-35.
*Design:* Qualitative phenomenological; 15 seniors on outer islands. *Key findings:* Preference for ageing in place; mosque-centred social life; intergenerational reciprocity; inter-island transport as health barrier; loneliness uncommon (2/15). *Significance:* First study of rural elderly experience, providing essential urban-rural contrast. https://ijsri.villacollege.edu.mv/index.php/ijsri/article/view/109

**Taj, Amir and Suzana, Mariyam (2023).** "Satisfaction of Caretakers towards Government's Home Visit Program for Bedridden Patients." *The Maldives National Journal of Research* 11(1): 53-66.
*Design:* Quantitative descriptive; 123 caretakers, February-July 2022. *Key findings:* Mean patient age 81.4; 79% female; 78% caretakers untrained; high overall satisfaction but very low satisfaction with procedures and training. *Significance:* Only evaluation of the home visit programme.

**Moosa, S. (2016).** "The wellbeing and social connectedness of older people in the small island developing state (SIDS) of Maldives." Unpublished doctoral dissertation, University of Waikato.
*Design:* Mixed methods. *Key findings:* Social engagement as protective factor; age as risk factor for social withdrawal. *Significance:* First doctoral research on Maldivian elderly wellbeing.

### Policy Documents and Government Reports

**Ministry of Social and Family Development (2024).** "National Ageing Policy" (Final Draft, 28 August 2024). Four policy areas, nine guiding principles, implementation framework with MoSFD as coordinator.

**Ministry of Social and Family Development (2024).** "Considerations for Developing Policies on Population Ageing in Maldives" (Presentation by Aminath Namza, 10 September 2024). Key demographic indicators, NCD data, policy framework.

**MoGFSS (2021).** "Voluntary National Survey on MIPAA Implementation." 25-page comprehensive self-assessment: 270 elder abuse cases, 67 home visit patients, 3 CSOs, self-reported capacity constraints.

**MoGFSS (2018).** "Promotion and Protection of the Rights of Children, Women, Elderly and Persons with Disabilities 2013-2018." Historical record of National Elderly Policy launch, Ranveyla campaign, home visit programme establishment.

**Aishath Mohamed Didi (2022).** Statement at High Level Panel Discussion of MIPAA Fourth Review, Bangkok. Six rights, National Plan of Action, gender dimensions.

### International Assessments

**HelpAge International (2023).** "Maldives: Population Ageing – Policy Case Study." 10-page assessment by Prof. Dissanayake. Monthly pension beneficiary data 2020; historical trends 2011-2019; pre-2009 system analysis; National Transfer Accounts findings.

**IMF (2024).** Article IV Consultation, Country Report No. 24/106. Fiscal deficit 12.2% of GDP; FAD pension sustainability assessment.

**OECD/IOPS (2025).** Maldives Country Profile. Housing collateralisation and Hajj scheme features.

**ADB (2012).** "Republic of the Maldives: Updating and Improving the Social Protection Index." SPI 0.073; 80% of expenditure to non-poor.

---


## 7.113 Conclusion: Five Non-Negotiable Reforms


The expanded evidence base reviewed in this chapter – drawing on seven primary research sources, multiple government policy documents, international assessments, and demographic projections – compels the identification of five non-negotiable reforms without which the Maldives will face a care crisis within a single generation:

**1. Enact the Elderly Rights Act.** The legislative gap identified in the MIPAA survey remains unfilled. Without a dedicated law, there is no mandatory reporting of elder abuse, no legally mandated care standards, no age-friendly building requirements, and no enforcement mechanism. The law must address the pension manipulation form of abuse identified in the evidence.

**2. Resolve the Pension Sustainability Crisis.** With expenditure at MVR 1.8 billion (2024) and projected to reach MVR 5.13 billion by 2054, the pension system requires urgent actuarial review, elimination of double-pensioning, and long-term fiscal planning. The IMF's direct engagement on pension sustainability signals the severity of the challenge. Delay increases the eventual adjustment cost.

**3. Scale Community-Based Care Infrastructure.** The Isravvehinge Naadhee model and the N. Kudafari example demonstrate viable approaches. These must be scaled to every inhabited island, with adaptation to local contexts. The Hattah programme and Ranveyla campaign provide programmatic frameworks. The Home Visit Program must expand from 67 patients (as of 2022) to every bedridden elderly person in the country.

**4. Address the Elderly Mental Health Crisis.** With 56.1% depression and 55.4% loneliness among Male' elderly (Azeez et al. 2020), population-level mental health intervention is needed – not optional wellness programming. Screening for depression and loneliness should be integrated into primary healthcare. The National Dementia Strategy must be completed and funded.

**5. Close the Urban-Rural Care Divide.** The urban-rural contrast documented by Nazra (2018), Azeez et al. (2020), and Jaufar & Ibrahim (2025) reveals that Male' is failing its elderly far more acutely than outer islands – despite having vastly more resources. Urban elderly policy must address high-rise isolation, elevator reliability, community space creation, and social housing eligibility. Rural elderly policy must address inter-island transport, mobile health services, and homecare workforce development.

The Maldives' demographic clock – with the "ageing society" threshold of 2031 now less than five years away – leaves no room for incremental adjustment. The revised National Ageing Policy (2024) and National Action Plan on Ageing (2025-2028) provide the policy architecture. What remains is implementation at a speed and scale commensurate with the demographic transformation that is already underway.

---

[^394]: Moosa, S. (2016). *The wellbeing and social connectedness of older people in the small island developing state (SIDS) of Maldives* (Unpublished doctoral dissertation). University of Waikato, New Zealand. https://waikato.researchgateway.ac.nz/bitstream/handle/10289/9967/thesis.pdf?sequence=3&isAllowed=y. Published findings in: Moosa, S. (2019). "Social Connectedness and Wellbeing of Ageing Populations in Small Islands," in *Social Isolation - An Interdisciplinary View*, ed. R. Morese, S. Palermo, and R. Fiorella (IntechOpen), DOI: 10.5772/intechopen.85351. Cited in Nazra (2018), p. 39, 42, 56.

[^395]: Moosa, S. (2019), "Social Connectedness and Wellbeing of Ageing Populations in Small Islands," IntechOpen, DOI: 10.5772/intechopen.85351, Chapter Metrics: 1,090 downloads. Moosa, S. and Koopman-Boyden, P.A. (2016). "Method for recruiting participants from isolated small island developing states (SIDS) for survey research," *Field Methods* 28, no. 4: 50-63.

[^396]: Moosa (2019), Section 2 (Concept and Methods): sample of 393, 31 islands, ethics approval from National Health Research Committee; wellbeing and social connectedness measures described.

[^397]: Moosa (2019), Table 1 and Section 3: Pearson correlation statistics showing overall social connectedness r=0.561**, r²=0.289 (29% shared contribution with wellbeing); family r=0.506**, r²=0.096; friends r=0.437**, r²=0.134.

[^398]: Moosa (2019), Table 2 and Section 3: detailed correlational analysis of 12 variables with wellbeing and satisfaction with overall social connectedness. Family contact frequency as most important contributor (r²=0.131 for satisfaction with overall social connectedness). Family contact type NOT significant for wellbeing (p=0.604).

[^399]: Moosa (2019), Sections 3.1 and 3.2: large network structures (5+ family, 1-4 friends); family contacts number r²=0.038 vs friends contacts number r²=0.088 for wellbeing; selectivity in friendships; combination of family and friends optimal.

[^400]: Moosa (2019), Section 3.2: social support from family positive contribution (4%) to wellbeing, contrasting with industrialised societies. Cultural expectation of family care normalises dependence.

[^401]: Moosa (2019), Section 3.1: religious social activity r²=0.078 for wellbeing; low community engagement attributed to collectivist prioritisation of family; cultural norm of not including elderly in community activities as sign of "respect."

[^402]: Moosa (2019), Sections 3.1 and 3.3: SIDS-specific findings – geographic dispersion, compensation through community, absence of formal associations on islands, cultural beliefs, limited amenities.

[^403]: Policy implications derived from Moosa (2019) findings on contact frequency vs network size, family/friend complementarity, outer island gaps, cultural exclusion, and conditional family support.

[^404]: Moosa, S. and Koopman-Boyden, P.A. (2016). "Method for recruiting participants from isolated small island developing states (SIDS) for survey research," *Field Methods* 28, no. 4: 50-63.

See also Clark et al., "Mapping Levels of Palliative Care Development in 198 Countries: The Situation in 2017," *Journal of Pain and Symptom Management* (2019), PMC7105817.
